<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Baseball</h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">

<p><b>Baseball</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Bat-and-ball_games" title="Bat-and-ball games">bat-and-ball game</a> played between two teams of nine players each, who take turns <a href="/wiki/Batting_(baseball)" title="Batting (baseball)">batting</a> and fielding.</p>
<p>The batting team attempts to score <a href="/wiki/Run_(baseball)" title="Run (baseball)">runs</a> by hitting a <a href="/wiki/Baseball_(ball)" title="Baseball (ball)">ball</a> that is thrown by the <a href="/wiki/Pitcher" title="Pitcher">pitcher</a> with a <a href="/wiki/Baseball_bat" title="Baseball bat">bat</a> swung by the batter, then running counter-clockwise around a series of four <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Base_(baseball)" title="Base (baseball)">bases</a>: first, second, third, and home plate. A run is scored when a player <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baserunning" title="Baserunning">advances around the bases</a> and returns to home plate.</p>
<p>Players on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Batting_team_(baseball)" title="Batting team (baseball)">batting team</a> take turns hitting against the pitcher of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fielding_team_(baseball)" title="Fielding team (baseball)">fielding team</a>, which tries to prevent runs by getting hitters <a href="/wiki/Out_(baseball)" title="Out (baseball)">out</a> in any of several ways. A player on the batting team who reaches a base <a href="/wiki/Safe_(baseball)" title="Safe (baseball)">safely</a> can later attempt to advance to subsequent bases during teammates' turns batting, such as on a <a href="/wiki/Hit_(baseball)" title="Hit (baseball)">hit</a> or by other means. The teams switch between batting and fielding whenever the fielding team records three outs. One turn batting for both teams, beginning with the visiting team, constitutes an <a href="/wiki/Inning" title="Inning">inning</a>. A game is composed of nine innings, and the team with the greater number of runs at the end of the game wins. Baseball has no game clock, although almost all games end in the ninth inning.</p>
<p>Baseball evolved from older bat-and-ball games already being played in England by the mid-18th century. This game was brought by immigrants to North America, <a href="/wiki/History_of_baseball_in_the_United_States" title="History of baseball in the United States">where the modern version developed</a>. By the late 19th century, baseball was widely recognized as the <a href="/wiki/National_sport" title="National sport">national sport</a> of the United States. Baseball is now popular in North America and parts of Central and South America, the Caribbean, and East Asia.</p>
<p>In the United States and Canada, professional <a href="/wiki/Major_League_Baseball" title="Major League Baseball">Major League Baseball</a> (MLB) teams are divided into the <a href="/wiki/National_League" title="National League">National League</a> (NL) and <a href="/wiki/American_League" title="American League">American League</a> (AL), each with three divisions: East, West, and Central. The major league champion is determined by <a href="/wiki/Major_League_Baseball_postseason" title="Major League Baseball postseason">playoffs</a> that culminate in the <a href="/wiki/World_Series" title="World Series">World Series</a>. The top level of play is similarly split in Japan between the <a href="/wiki/Central_League" title="Central League">Central</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pacific_League" title="Pacific League">Pacific Leagues</a> and in Cuba between the <a href="/wiki/Cuban_National_Series" title="Cuban National Series">West League and East League</a>.</p>
<p></p>

<li><a href="/wiki/Baseball_in_South_Korea" title="Baseball in South Korea">South Korea</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Baseball_in_the_Netherlands" title="Baseball in the Netherlands">Netherlands</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/History_of_baseball_in_Nicaragua" title="History of baseball in Nicaragua">Nicaragua</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Baseball_in_Palau" title="Baseball in Palau">Palau</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Baseball_in_the_Philippines" title="Baseball in the Philippines">Philippines</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Baseball_in_Puerto_Rico" title="Baseball in Puerto Rico">Puerto Rico</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Baseball_in_Spain" title="Baseball in Spain">Spain</a></li>

<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Medieval_baseball_(El_juego_de_la_Pelota)_in_the_Cantigas_de_Santa_Maria.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1600" data-file-width="1553" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Medieval_baseball_%28El_juego_de_la_Pelota%29_in_the_Cantigas_de_Santa_Maria.jpg/220px-Medieval_baseball_%28El_juego_de_la_Pelota%29_in_the_Cantigas_de_Santa_Maria.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Medieval_baseball_%28El_juego_de_la_Pelota%29_in_the_Cantigas_de_Santa_Maria.jpg/330px-Medieval_baseball_%28El_juego_de_la_Pelota%29_in_the_Cantigas_de_Santa_Maria.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Medieval_baseball_%28El_juego_de_la_Pelota%29_in_the_Cantigas_de_Santa_Maria.jpg/440px-Medieval_baseball_%28El_juego_de_la_Pelota%29_in_the_Cantigas_de_Santa_Maria.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Medieval_baseball_(El_juego_de_la_Pelota)_in_the_Cantigas_de_Santa_Maria.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A game from the <a href="/wiki/Cantigas_de_Santa_Maria" title="Cantigas de Santa Maria">Cantigas de Santa Maria</a>, c. 1280, involving tossing a ball, hitting it with a stick and competing with others to catch it.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The evolution of baseball from older bat-and-ball games is difficult to trace with precision. A <a href="/wiki/French_people" title="French people">French</a> manuscript from 1344 contains an illustration of clerics playing a game, possibly <i><a href="/wiki/La_soule" title="La soule">la soule</a></i>, with similarities to baseball.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> Other old French games such as <i>thèque</i>, <i>la balle au bâton</i>, and <i>la balle empoisonnée</i> also appear to be related.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> Consensus once held that today's baseball is a North American development from the older game <a href="/wiki/Rounders" title="Rounders">rounders</a>, popular in Great Britain and Ireland. <i><a href="/wiki/Baseball_Before_We_Knew_It" title="Baseball Before We Knew It">Baseball Before We Knew It</a>: A Search for the Roots of the Game</i> (2005), by David Block, suggests that the game originated in England; recently uncovered historical evidence supports this position. Block argues that rounders and early baseball were actually regional variants of each other, and that the game's most direct antecedents are the English games of <a href="/wiki/Stoolball" title="Stoolball">stoolball</a> and "tut-ball."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> It has long been believed that <a href="/wiki/Cricket" title="Cricket">cricket</a> also descended from such games, though evidence uncovered in early 2009 suggests that cricket may have been imported to England from <a href="/wiki/Flanders" title="Flanders">Flanders</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<p>The earliest known reference to baseball is in a 1744 British publication, <i><a href="/wiki/A_Little_Pretty_Pocket-Book" title="A Little Pretty Pocket-Book">A Little Pretty Pocket-Book</a></i>, by <a href="/wiki/John_Newbery" title="John Newbery">John Newbery</a>. It contains a rhymed description of "base-ball" and a <a href="/wiki/Woodcut" title="Woodcut">woodcut</a> that shows a field set-up somewhat similar to the modern game—though in a triangular rather than diamond configuration, and with posts instead of ground-level bases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/David_Block" title="David Block">David Block</a> discovered that the first recorded game of "Bass-Ball" took place in 1749 in <a href="/wiki/Surrey" title="Surrey">Surrey</a>, and featured the <a href="/wiki/Frederick,_Prince_of_Wales" title="Frederick, Prince of Wales">Prince of Wales</a> as a player.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> William Bray, an English lawyer, recorded a game of baseball on <a href="/wiki/Easter" title="Easter">Easter</a> Monday 1755 in <a href="/wiki/Guildford" title="Guildford">Guildford</a>, <a href="/wiki/Surrey" title="Surrey">Surrey</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> This early form of the game was apparently brought to Canada by English immigrants. Rounders was also brought to the USA by Canadians of both British and Irish ancestry. The first known American reference to baseball appears in a 1791 <a href="/wiki/Pittsfield,_Massachusetts" title="Pittsfield, Massachusetts">Pittsfield, Massachusetts</a>, town bylaw prohibiting the playing of the game near the town's new meeting house.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> By 1796, a version of the game was well-known enough to earn a mention in a German scholar's book on popular pastimes. As described by Johann Gutsmuths, "<i>englische Base-ball</i>" involved a contest between two teams, in which "the batter has three attempts to hit the ball while at the home plate." Only one out was required to retire a side.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Alexander_Cartwright,_photograph_by_J._J._Williams_(PP-69-3-005).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1210" data-file-width="837" height="217" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Alexander_Cartwright%2C_photograph_by_J._J._Williams_%28PP-69-3-005%29.jpg/150px-Alexander_Cartwright%2C_photograph_by_J._J._Williams_%28PP-69-3-005%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Alexander_Cartwright%2C_photograph_by_J._J._Williams_%28PP-69-3-005%29.jpg/225px-Alexander_Cartwright%2C_photograph_by_J._J._Williams_%28PP-69-3-005%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Alexander_Cartwright%2C_photograph_by_J._J._Williams_%28PP-69-3-005%29.jpg/300px-Alexander_Cartwright%2C_photograph_by_J._J._Williams_%28PP-69-3-005%29.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Alexander_Cartwright,_photograph_by_J._J._Williams_(PP-69-3-005).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Alexander Cartwright, father of modern baseball</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>By the early 1830s, there were reports of a variety of uncodified bat-and-ball games recognizable as early forms of baseball being played around North America. These games were often referred to locally as "<a href="/wiki/Town_ball" title="Town ball">town ball</a>", though other names such as "round-ball" and "base-ball" were also used.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> Among the earliest examples to receive a detailed description—albeit five decades after the fact, in a letter from an attendee to <i><a href="/wiki/Sporting_Life_(American_newspaper)" title="Sporting Life (American newspaper)">Sporting Life</a></i> magazine—took place in Beachville, <a href="/wiki/Ontario" title="Ontario">Ontario</a>, in 1838. There were many similarities to modern baseball, and some crucial differences: five bases (or <i>byes</i>); first bye just 18 feet (5.5 m) from the home bye; batter out if a hit ball was caught after the first bounce.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Doubleday_myth" title="Doubleday myth">once widely accepted story</a> that <a href="/wiki/Abner_Doubleday" title="Abner Doubleday">Abner Doubleday</a> invented baseball in <a href="/wiki/Cooperstown,_New_York" title="Cooperstown, New York">Cooperstown, New York</a>, in 1839 has been conclusively debunked by sports historians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1845, <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Cartwright" title="Alexander Cartwright">Alexander Cartwright</a>, a member of New York City's <a href="/wiki/Knickerbocker_Club" title="Knickerbocker Club">Knickerbocker Club</a>, led the codification of the so-called <a href="/wiki/Knickerbocker_Rules" title="Knickerbocker Rules">Knickerbocker Rules</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> The practice, common to bat-and-ball games of the day, of "soaking" or "plugging"—effecting a <a href="/wiki/Putout" title="Putout">putout</a> by hitting a runner with a thrown ball—was barred. The rules thus facilitated the use of a smaller, harder ball than had been common. Several other rules also brought the Knickerbockers' game close to the modern one, though a ball caught on the first bounce was, again, an out and only underhand pitching was allowed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> While there are reports that the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Knickerbockers" title="New York Knickerbockers">New York Knickerbockers</a> played games in 1845, the contest long recognized as the first officially recorded baseball game in U.S. history took place on June 19, 1846, in <a href="/wiki/Hoboken,_New_Jersey" title="Hoboken, New Jersey">Hoboken, New Jersey</a>: the "New York Nine" defeated the Knickerbockers, 23–1, in four innings<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> (three earlier games have recently been discovered). With the Knickerbocker code as the basis, the rules of modern baseball continued to evolve over the next half-century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="History_of_baseball_in_the_United_States">History of baseball in the United States</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Baseball_in_the_United_States" title="Baseball in the United States">Baseball in the United States</a> and <a href="/wiki/History_of_baseball_in_the_United_States" title="History of baseball in the United States">History of baseball in the United States</a></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="The_game_turns_professional">The game turns professional</span></h4>
<p>In the mid-1850s, a baseball craze hit the <a href="/wiki/New_York_metropolitan_area" title="New York metropolitan area">New York metropolitan area</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> By 1856, local journals were referring to baseball as the "national pastime" or "national game."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> A year later, sixteen area clubs formed the sport's first governing body, the <a href="/wiki/National_Association_of_Base_Ball_Players" title="National Association of Base Ball Players">National Association of Base Ball Players</a>. In 1858 in <a href="/wiki/Corona,_Queens" title="Corona, Queens">Corona, Queens</a> <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York</a>, at the Fashion Race Course, the first games of baseball to charge admission took place. The games, which took place between the all stars of <a href="/wiki/Brooklyn" title="Brooklyn">Brooklyn</a>, including players from the <a href="/wiki/Brooklyn_Atlantics" title="Brooklyn Atlantics">Brooklyn Atlantics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Excelsior_of_Brooklyn" title="Excelsior of Brooklyn">Excelsior of Brooklyn</a>, Putnams and <a href="/wiki/Eckford_of_Brooklyn" title="Eckford of Brooklyn">Eckford of Brooklyn</a>, and the All Stars of New York (<a href="/wiki/Manhattan" title="Manhattan">Manhattan</a>), including players from the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Knickerbockers" title="New York Knickerbockers">New York Knickerbockers</a>, Gothams (predecessors of the <a href="/wiki/San_Francisco_Giants" title="San Francisco Giants">San Francisco Giants</a>), Eagles and Empire, are commonly believed to be the first all-star baseball games.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> In 1863, the organization disallowed putouts made by catching a <a href="/wiki/Fair_ball" title="Fair ball">fair ball</a> on the first bounce. Four years later, it barred participation by <a href="/wiki/African_Americans" title="African Americans">African Americans</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> The game's commercial potential was developing: in 1869 the first fully professional baseball club, the <a href="/wiki/Cincinnati_Red_Stockings" title="Cincinnati Red Stockings">Cincinnati Red Stockings</a>, was formed and went undefeated against a schedule of semipro and amateur teams.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> The first professional league, the <a href="/wiki/National_Association_of_Professional_Base_Ball_Players" title="National Association of Professional Base Ball Players">National Association of Professional Base Ball Players</a>, lasted from 1871 to 1875; scholars dispute <a href="/wiki/National_Association_as_a_major_league" title="National Association as a major league">its status as a major league</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<p>The more formally structured <a href="/wiki/National_League" title="National League">National League</a> was founded in 1876. As the oldest surviving major league, the National League is sometimes referred to as the "senior circuit."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> Several other major leagues formed and failed. In 1884, African American <a href="/wiki/Moses_Fleetwood_Walker" title="Moses Fleetwood Walker">Moses Walker</a> (and, briefly, his brother Welday) played in one of these, the <a href="/wiki/American_Association_(19th_century)" title="American Association (19th century)">American Association</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> An injury ended Walker's major league career, and by the early 1890s, a <a href="/wiki/Gentlemen%27s_agreement" title="Gentlemen's agreement">gentlemen's agreement</a> in the form of the <a href="/wiki/Baseball_color_line" title="Baseball color line">baseball color line</a> effectively barred black players from the white-owned professional leagues, major and minor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> Professional <a href="/wiki/Negro_league_baseball" title="Negro league baseball">Negro leagues</a> formed, but quickly folded. Several independent African American teams succeeded as <a href="/wiki/Barnstorm_(sports)" title="Barnstorm (sports)">barnstormers</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> Also in 1884, overhand pitching was legalized.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-R71_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-R71-29">[29]</a></sup> In 1887, <a href="/wiki/Softball" title="Softball">softball</a>, under the name of indoor baseball or indoor-outdoor, was invented as a winter version of the parent game.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> Virtually all of the modern baseball rules were in place by 1893; the last major change—counting <a href="/wiki/Foul_ball" title="Foul ball">foul balls</a> as <a href="/wiki/Strike_zone" title="Strike zone">strikes</a>—was instituted in 1901.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-R71_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-R71-29">[29]</a></sup> The National League's first successful counterpart, the <a href="/wiki/American_League" title="American League">American League</a>, which evolved from the minor <a href="/wiki/Western_League_(original)" title="Western League (original)">Western League</a>, was established that year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> The two leagues, each with eight teams, were rivals that fought for the best players, often disregarding each other's contracts and engaging in bitter legal disputes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:New_York_Giants_Opening_Day_line-up_at_the_Polo_Grounds_New_York._Left_to_right_Fred_Snodgrass,_Tillie_Shafer,_George_Burns,_Larry_Doyle,_Red_Murray,_Fred_Merkle,_Buck_Herzog,_Chief_Meyers_(baseball)_(LOC).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="697" data-file-width="939" height="163" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/New_York_Giants_Opening_Day_line-up_at_the_Polo_Grounds_New_York._Left_to_right_Fred_Snodgrass%2C_Tillie_Shafer%2C_George_Burns%2C_Larry_Doyle%2C_Red_Murray%2C_Fred_Merkle%2C_Buck_Herzog%2C_Chief_Meyers_%28baseball%29_%28LOC%29.jpg/220px-thumbnail.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/New_York_Giants_Opening_Day_line-up_at_the_Polo_Grounds_New_York._Left_to_right_Fred_Snodgrass%2C_Tillie_Shafer%2C_George_Burns%2C_Larry_Doyle%2C_Red_Murray%2C_Fred_Merkle%2C_Buck_Herzog%2C_Chief_Meyers_%28baseball%29_%28LOC%29.jpg/330px-thumbnail.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/New_York_Giants_Opening_Day_line-up_at_the_Polo_Grounds_New_York._Left_to_right_Fred_Snodgrass%2C_Tillie_Shafer%2C_George_Burns%2C_Larry_Doyle%2C_Red_Murray%2C_Fred_Merkle%2C_Buck_Herzog%2C_Chief_Meyers_%28baseball%29_%28LOC%29.jpg/440px-thumbnail.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:New_York_Giants_Opening_Day_line-up_at_the_Polo_Grounds_New_York._Left_to_right_Fred_Snodgrass,_Tillie_Shafer,_George_Burns,_Larry_Doyle,_Red_Murray,_Fred_Merkle,_Buck_Herzog,_Chief_Meyers_(baseball)_(LOC).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_York_Giants_(NL)" title="New York Giants (NL)">New York Giants</a> baseball team, 1913. <a href="/wiki/Fred_Merkle" title="Fred Merkle">Fred Merkle</a>, sixth in line, committed a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baserunning" title="Baserunning">baserunning</a> gaffe in a crucial 1908 game that became famous as <a href="/wiki/Merkle%27s_Boner" title="Merkle's Boner">Merkle's Boner</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A modicum of peace was eventually established, leading to the National Agreement of 1903. The pact formalized relations both between the two major leagues and between them and the National Association of Professional Base Ball Leagues, representing most of the country's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Minor_league_baseball" title="Minor league baseball">minor professional leagues</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/World_Series" title="World Series">World Series</a>, pitting the two major league champions against each other, was inaugurated that fall, albeit without express major league sanction: The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Boston_Americans" title="Boston Americans">Boston Americans</a> of the American League defeated the <a href="/wiki/Pittsburgh_Pirates" title="Pittsburgh Pirates">Pittsburgh Pirates</a> of the National League.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> The next year, the series was not held, as the National League champion <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_York_Giants_(NL)" title="New York Giants (NL)">New York Giants</a>, under <a href="/wiki/Manager_(baseball)" title="Manager (baseball)">manager</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/John_McGraw_(baseball)" title="John McGraw (baseball)">John McGraw</a>, refused to recognize the major league status of the American League and its champion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> In 1905, the Giants were National League champions again and team management relented, leading to the establishment of the World Series as the major leagues' annual championship event.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup></p>
<p>As professional baseball became increasingly profitable, players frequently raised grievances against owners over issues of control and equitable income distribution. During the major leagues' early decades, players on various teams occasionally attempted strikes, which routinely failed when their jobs were sufficiently threatened. In general, the strict rules of baseball contracts and the <a href="/wiki/Reserve_clause" title="Reserve clause">reserve clause</a>, which bound players to their teams even when their contracts had ended, tended to keep the players in check.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> Motivated by dislike for particularly stingy owner <a href="/wiki/Charles_Comiskey" title="Charles Comiskey">Charles Comiskey</a> and gamblers' payoffs, real and promised, members of the <a href="/wiki/Chicago_White_Sox" title="Chicago White Sox">Chicago White Sox</a> conspired to <a href="/wiki/Match_fixing" title="Match fixing">throw</a> the <a href="/wiki/1919_World_Series" title="1919 World Series">1919 World Series</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Black_Sox_Scandal" title="Black Sox Scandal">Black Sox Scandal</a> led to the formation of a new National Commission of baseball that drew the two major leagues closer together.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> The first <a href="/wiki/Commissioner_of_Baseball" title="Commissioner of Baseball">major league baseball commissioner</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kenesaw_Mountain_Landis" title="Kenesaw Mountain Landis">Kenesaw Mountain Landis</a>, was elected in 1920. That year also saw the founding of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Negro_National_League_(1920%E2%80%931931)" title="Negro National League (1920–1931)">Negro National League</a>; the first significant Negro league, it would operate until 1931. For part of the 1920s, it was joined by the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Colored_League" title="Eastern Colored League">Eastern Colored League</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup></p>
<p>Professional baseball was played in northeastern cities with a large immigrant-ethnic population; they gave strong support to the new sport. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Irish_American" title="Irish American">The Irish Catholics</a> dominated in the late 19th century, comprising a third or more of the players and many of the top stars and managers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> Historian Jerrold Casway argues that:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Baseball for Irish kids was a shortcut to the American dream and to self-indulgent glory and fortune. By the mid-1880s these young Irish men dominated the sport and popularized a style of play that was termed heady, daring, and spontaneous.... <a href="/wiki/Ed_Delahanty" title="Ed Delahanty">Ed Delahanty</a> personified the flamboyant, exciting spectator-favorite, the Casey-at-the-bat, Irish slugger. The handsome masculine athlete who is expected to live as large as he played.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Rise_of_Ruth_and_racial_integration">Rise of Ruth and racial integration</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Babe_Ruth2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3456" data-file-width="2623" height="224" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Babe_Ruth2.jpg/170px-Babe_Ruth2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Babe_Ruth2.jpg/255px-Babe_Ruth2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Babe_Ruth2.jpg/340px-Babe_Ruth2.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Babe_Ruth2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Babe_Ruth" title="Babe Ruth">Babe Ruth</a> in 1920, the year he joined the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Yankees" title="New York Yankees">New York Yankees</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Compared with the present, professional baseball in the early 20th century was lower-scoring and pitchers, the likes of <a href="/wiki/Walter_Johnson" title="Walter Johnson">Walter Johnson</a> and <a href="/wiki/Christy_Mathewson" title="Christy Mathewson">Christy Mathewson</a>, were more dominant. The "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inside_Baseball_(strategy)" title="Inside Baseball (strategy)">inside game</a>," which demanded that players "scratch for runs", was played much more aggressively than it is today: the brilliant and often violent <a href="/wiki/Ty_Cobb" title="Ty Cobb">Ty Cobb</a> epitomized this style.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> The so-called <a href="/wiki/Dead-ball_era" title="Dead-ball era">dead-ball era</a> ended in the early 1920s with several changes in rule and circumstance that were advantageous to hitters. Strict new regulations governing the ball's size, shape and composition along with a new rule officially banning the <a href="/wiki/Spitball" title="Spitball">spitball</a>, along with other pitches that depended on the ball being treated or roughed-up with foreign substances after the death of <a href="/wiki/Ray_Chapman" title="Ray Chapman">Ray Chapman</a> who was hit by a pitch in August 1920, coupled with superior materials available after World War I, resulted in a ball that traveled farther when hit. The construction of additional seating to accommodate the rising popularity of the game often had the effect of bringing the outfield fences closer in, making <a href="/wiki/Home_run" title="Home run">home runs</a> more common.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> The rise of the legendary player <a href="/wiki/Babe_Ruth" title="Babe Ruth">Babe Ruth</a>, the first great power hitter of the new era, helped permanently alter the nature of the game. The club with which Ruth set most of his slugging records, the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Yankees" title="New York Yankees">New York Yankees</a>, built a reputation as the majors' premier team.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> In the late 1920s and early 1930s, <a href="/wiki/St._Louis_Cardinals" title="St. Louis Cardinals">St. Louis Cardinals</a> <a href="/wiki/General_manager_(baseball)" title="General manager (baseball)">general manager</a> <a href="/wiki/Branch_Rickey" title="Branch Rickey">Branch Rickey</a> invested in several <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Minor_league_baseball" title="Minor league baseball">minor league clubs</a> and developed the first modern "farm system".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> A new <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Negro_National_League_(1933%E2%80%931948)" title="Negro National League (1933–1948)">Negro National League</a> was organized in 1933; four years later, it was joined by the <a href="/wiki/Negro_American_League" title="Negro American League">Negro American League</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Baseball_Hall_of_Fame_balloting,_1936" title="Baseball Hall of Fame balloting, 1936">first elections</a> to the <a href="/wiki/National_Baseball_Hall_of_Fame_and_Museum" title="National Baseball Hall of Fame and Museum">Baseball Hall of Fame</a> took place in 1936. In 1939 <a href="/wiki/Little_League_Baseball" title="Little League Baseball">Little League Baseball</a> was founded in Pennsylvania. By the late 1940s, it was the organizing body for <a href="/wiki/Amateur_baseball_in_the_United_States" title="Amateur baseball in the United States">children's baseball leagues</a> across the United States.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:142px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:JackieRobinson1945.jpg"><img alt="Robinson posing in the uniform cap of the Kansas City Royals, a California Winter League barnstorming team, November 1945 (photo by Maurice Terrell)" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="492" data-file-width="266" height="259" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/JackieRobinson1945.jpg/140px-JackieRobinson1945.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/JackieRobinson1945.jpg/210px-JackieRobinson1945.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/43/JackieRobinson1945.jpg 2x" width="140"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:JackieRobinson1945.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Jackie_Robinson" title="Jackie Robinson">Jackie Robinson</a> in 1945, with the era's <a href="/wiki/Kansas_City_Royals" title="Kansas City Royals">Kansas City Royals</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Barnstorm_(sports)" title="Barnstorm (sports)">barnstorming</a> squad associated with the <a href="/wiki/Negro_American_League" title="Negro American League">Negro American League</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Kansas_City_Monarchs" title="Kansas City Monarchs">Kansas City Monarchs</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>With America's entry into World War II, many professional players had left to serve in the armed forces. A large number of minor league teams disbanded as a result and the major league game seemed under threat as well. <a href="/wiki/Chicago_Cubs" title="Chicago Cubs">Chicago Cubs</a> owner <a href="/wiki/Philip_K._Wrigley" title="Philip K. Wrigley">Philip K. Wrigley</a> led the formation of a new professional league with women players to help keep the game in the public eye – the <a href="/wiki/All-American_Girls_Professional_Baseball_League" title="All-American Girls Professional Baseball League">All-American Girls Professional Baseball League</a> existed from 1943 to 1954.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> The inaugural <a href="/wiki/College_World_Series" title="College World Series">College World Series</a> was held in 1947, and the Babe Ruth League youth program was founded. This program soon became another important organizing body for children's baseball. The first crack in the unwritten agreement barring blacks from white-controlled professional ball occurred the previous year: <a href="/wiki/Jackie_Robinson" title="Jackie Robinson">Jackie Robinson</a> was signed by the National League's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brooklyn_Dodgers" title="Brooklyn Dodgers">Brooklyn Dodgers</a>—where Branch Rickey had become general manager—and began playing for their minor league team in Montreal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> In 1947, Robinson broke the major leagues' color barrier when he debuted with the Dodgers. <a href="/wiki/Larry_Doby" title="Larry Doby">Larry Doby</a> debuted with the American League's <a href="/wiki/Cleveland_Indians" title="Cleveland Indians">Cleveland Indians</a> the same year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> Latin American players, largely overlooked before, also started entering the majors in greater numbers. In 1951, two Chicago White Sox, Venezuelan-born <a href="/wiki/Chico_Carrasquel" title="Chico Carrasquel">Chico Carrasquel</a> and black Cuban-born <a href="/wiki/Minnie_Mi%C3%B1oso" title="Minnie Miñoso">Minnie Miñoso</a>, became the first Hispanic <a href="/wiki/Major_League_Baseball_All-Star_Game" title="Major League Baseball All-Star Game">All-Stars</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-P111_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-P111-49">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
<p>Facing competition as varied as television and <a href="/wiki/American_football" title="American football">football</a>, baseball attendance at all levels declined. While the majors rebounded by the mid-1950s, the minor leagues were gutted and hundreds of semipro and amateur teams dissolved.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Simm_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-Simm-52">[52]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Racial_integration" title="Racial integration">Integration</a> proceeded slowly: by 1953, only six of the 16 major league teams had a black player on the roster.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-P111_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-P111-49">[49]</a></sup> That year, the <a href="/wiki/Major_League_Baseball_Players_Association" title="Major League Baseball Players Association">Major League Baseball Players Association</a> was founded. It was the first professional baseball union to survive more than briefly, but it remained largely ineffective for years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> No major league team had been located west of <a href="/wiki/St._Louis" title="St. Louis">St. Louis</a> until 1958, when the Brooklyn Dodgers and New York Giants relocated to <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles_Dodgers" title="Los Angeles Dodgers">Los Angeles</a> and <a href="/wiki/San_Francisco_Giants" title="San Francisco Giants">San Francisco</a>, respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> The majors' final all-white bastion, the <a href="/wiki/Boston_Red_Sox" title="Boston Red Sox">Boston Red Sox</a>, added a black player in 1959.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-P111_49-2"><a href="#cite_note-P111-49">[49]</a></sup> With the integration of the majors drying up the available pool of players, the last Negro league folded the following year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> In 1961, the American League reached the West Coast with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Los_Angeles_Angels" title="Los Angeles Angels">Los Angeles Angels</a> <a href="/wiki/Expansion_team" title="Expansion team">expansion team</a>, and the major league season was extended from 154 games to 162. This coincidentally helped <a href="/wiki/Roger_Maris" title="Roger Maris">Roger Maris</a> break Babe Ruth's long-standing single-season home run record, one of the most celebrated marks in baseball.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> Along with the Angels, three other new franchises were launched during 1961–62. With this, the first major league expansion in 60 years, each league now had ten teams.</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Attendance_records_and_the_age_of_steroids">Attendance records and the age of steroids</span></h4>
<p>The players' union became bolder under the leadership of former <a href="/wiki/United_Steelworkers" title="United Steelworkers">United Steelworkers</a> chief economist and negotiator <a href="/wiki/Marvin_Miller" title="Marvin Miller">Marvin Miller</a>, who was elected executive director in 1966.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> On the playing field, major league pitchers were becoming increasingly dominant again. After the 1968 season, in an effort to restore balance, the <a href="/wiki/Strike_zone" title="Strike zone">strike zone</a> was reduced and the height of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pitcher%27s_mound" title="Pitcher's mound">pitcher's mound</a> was lowered from 15 to 10 inches. In 1969, both the National and American leagues added two more expansion teams, the leagues were reorganized into two divisions each, and a post-season playoff system leading to the World Series was instituted. Also that same year, <a href="/wiki/Curt_Flood" title="Curt Flood">Curt Flood</a> of the St. Louis Cardinals made the first serious legal challenge to the reserve clause. The major leagues' <a href="/wiki/1972_Major_League_Baseball_strike" title="1972 Major League Baseball strike">first general players' strike</a> took place in 1972.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> In another effort to add more offense to the game, the American League adopted the <a href="/wiki/Designated_hitter" title="Designated hitter">designated hitter</a> rule the following year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> In 1975, the union's power—and players' salaries—began to increase greatly when the reserve clause was <a href="/wiki/Seitz_decision" title="Seitz decision">effectively struck down</a>, leading to the <a href="/wiki/Free_agent" title="Free agent">free agency system</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> In 1977, two more expansion teams joined the American League. Significant work stoppages occurred again in <a href="/wiki/1981_Major_League_Baseball_strike" title="1981 Major League Baseball strike">1981</a> and <a href="/wiki/1994%E2%80%9395_Major_League_Baseball_strike" title="1994–95 Major League Baseball strike">1994</a>, the latter forcing the cancellation of the World Series for the first time in 90 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> Attendance had been growing steadily since the mid-1970s and in 1994, before the stoppage, the majors were setting their all-time record for per-game attendance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Simm_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-Simm-52">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:D7K_4969_Roy_Halladay.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1716" data-file-width="2076" height="182" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/D7K_4969_Roy_Halladay.jpg/220px-D7K_4969_Roy_Halladay.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/D7K_4969_Roy_Halladay.jpg/330px-D7K_4969_Roy_Halladay.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/D7K_4969_Roy_Halladay.jpg/440px-D7K_4969_Roy_Halladay.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:D7K_4969_Roy_Halladay.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
In May 2010, the <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia_Phillies" title="Philadelphia Phillies">Philadelphia Phillies</a>' <a href="/wiki/Roy_Halladay" title="Roy Halladay">Roy Halladay</a> pitched the 20th major league <a href="/wiki/Perfect_game" title="Perfect game">perfect game</a>. That October, he pitched only the second <a href="/wiki/No-hitter" title="No-hitter">no-hitter</a> in MLB postseason history.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The addition of two more expansion teams after the 1993 season had facilitated another restructuring of the major leagues, this time into three divisions each. Offensive production—the number of home runs in particular—had surged that year, and again in the abbreviated 1994 season.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> After play resumed in 1995, this trend continued and non-division-winning <a href="/wiki/Wild_card_(sports)" title="Wild card (sports)">wild card</a> teams became a permanent fixture of the post-season. Regular-season <a href="/wiki/Interleague_play" title="Interleague play">interleague play</a> was introduced in 1997 and the second-highest attendance mark for a full season was set.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> The next year, <a href="/wiki/Mark_McGwire" title="Mark McGwire">Mark McGwire</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sammy_Sosa" title="Sammy Sosa">Sammy Sosa</a> <a href="/wiki/1998_Major_League_Baseball_home_run_record_chase" title="1998 Major League Baseball home run record chase">both surpassed</a> Maris's decades-old single season home run record and two more expansion franchises were added. In 2000, the National and American leagues were dissolved as legal entities. While their identities were maintained for scheduling purposes (and the designated hitter distinction), the regulations and other functions—such as player discipline and umpire supervision—they had administered separately were consolidated under the rubric of <a href="/wiki/Major_League_Baseball" title="Major League Baseball">Major League Baseball</a> (MLB).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2001, <a href="/wiki/Barry_Bonds" title="Barry Bonds">Barry Bonds</a> established the current record of 73 home runs in a single season. There had long been suspicions that the dramatic increase in power hitting was fueled in large part by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Banned_substances_in_baseball" title="Banned substances in baseball">the abuse of illegal steroids</a> (as well as by the dilution of pitching talent due to expansion), but the issue only began attracting significant media attention in 2002 and there was no penalty for the use of performance-enhancing drugs before 2004.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> In 2007, Bonds became MLB's all-time home run leader, surpassing <a href="/wiki/Hank_Aaron" title="Hank Aaron">Hank Aaron</a>, as total major league and minor league attendance both reached all-time highs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MajorAtRec_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-MajorAtRec-67">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MinorAtRec_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-MinorAtRec-68">[68]</a></sup> Even though McGwire, Sosa, and Bonds—as well as many other players, including storied pitcher <a href="/wiki/Roger_Clemens" title="Roger Clemens">Roger Clemens</a>—have been implicated in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Steroid_era" title="Steroid era">steroid abuse scandal</a>, their feats and those of other sluggers had become the major leagues' defining attraction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> In contrast to the professional game's resurgence in popularity after the 1994 interruption, Little League enrollment was in decline: after peaking in 1996, it dropped 1 percent a year over the following decade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> With more rigorous testing and penalties for performance-enhancing drug use a possible factor, the balance between bat and ball swung markedly in 2010, which became known as the "Year of the Pitcher".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> Runs per game fell to their lowest level in 18 years, and the strikeout rate was higher than it had been in half a century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup></p>
<p>Before the start of the 2012 season, MLB altered its rules to double the number of wild card teams admitted into the playoffs to two per league.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> The playoff expansion resulted in the addition of annual <a href="/wiki/Major_League_Baseball_wild-card_game" title="Major League Baseball wild-card game">one-game playoffs</a> between the wild card teams in each league.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Baseball_around_the_world">Baseball around the world<span id="Around-the-world"></span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_baseball_outside_the_United_States" title="History of baseball outside the United States">History of baseball outside the United States</a></div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Baseball_in_Cuba" title="Baseball in Cuba">Baseball in Cuba</a>, <a href="/wiki/Baseball_in_Japan" title="Baseball in Japan">Baseball in Japan</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Baseball_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Baseball in the United Kingdom">Baseball in the United Kingdom</a></div>
<p>Baseball, widely known as America's pastime, is well established in several other countries as well. The history of baseball in Canada has remained closely linked with that of the sport in the United States. As early as 1877, a professional league, the International Association, featured teams from both countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> While baseball is widely played in Canada and many minor league teams have been based in the country, the American major leagues did not include a Canadian club until 1969, when the <a href="/wiki/Montreal_Expos" title="Montreal Expos">Montreal Expos</a> joined the National League as an expansion team. In 1977, the expansion <a href="/wiki/Toronto_Blue_Jays" title="Toronto Blue Jays">Toronto Blue Jays</a> joined the American League. The Blue Jays won the World Series in 1992 and 1993, the first and still the only club from outside the United States to do so. After the 2004 season, Major League Baseball relocated the Expos to Washington, D.C., where the team is now known as the <a href="/wiki/Washington_Nationals" title="Washington Nationals">Nationals</a>.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:212px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:WBC2006_Sadaharu_Oh.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="555" data-file-width="355" height="328" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/WBC2006_Sadaharu_Oh.jpg/210px-WBC2006_Sadaharu_Oh.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/WBC2006_Sadaharu_Oh.jpg/315px-WBC2006_Sadaharu_Oh.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/8f/WBC2006_Sadaharu_Oh.jpg 2x" width="210"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:WBC2006_Sadaharu_Oh.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Sadaharu_Oh" title="Sadaharu Oh">Sadaharu Oh</a> managing the <a href="/wiki/Japan_national_baseball_team" title="Japan national baseball team">Japan national team</a> in the <a href="/wiki/2006_World_Baseball_Classic" title="2006 World Baseball Classic">2006 World Baseball Classic</a>. Playing for the <a href="/wiki/Central_League" title="Central League">Central League</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Yomiuri_Giants" title="Yomiuri Giants">Yomiuri Giants</a> (1959–80), Oh set the professional world record for home runs.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1847, American soldiers played what may have been the first baseball game in Mexico at <a href="/wiki/Parque_Los_Berros" title="Parque Los Berros">Parque Los Berros</a> in <a href="/wiki/Xalapa" title="Xalapa">Xalapa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Veracruz" title="Veracruz">Veracruz</a>. A few days after the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Cerro_Gordo" title="Battle of Cerro Gordo">Battle of Cerro Gordo</a>, they used the "wooden leg captured (by the Fourth Illinois regiment) from General <a href="/wiki/Antonio_L%C3%B3pez_de_Santa_Anna" title="Antonio López de Santa Anna">Santa Anna</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> The first formal baseball league outside of the United States and Canada was founded in 1878 in Cuba, which maintains a rich baseball tradition and whose national team has been one of the world's strongest since international play began in the late 1930s (all organized baseball in the country has officially been amateur since the <a href="/wiki/Cuban_Revolution" title="Cuban Revolution">Cuban Revolution</a>). The Dominican Republic held its first islandwide championship tournament in 1912.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> Professional baseball tournaments and leagues began to form in other countries between the world wars, including the Netherlands (formed in 1922), Australia (1934), Japan (1936), Mexico (1937), and Puerto Rico (1938).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Nippon_Professional_Baseball" title="Nippon Professional Baseball">Japanese major leagues</a>—the <a href="/wiki/Central_League" title="Central League">Central League</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pacific_League" title="Pacific League">Pacific League</a>—have long been considered the highest quality professional circuits outside of the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> Japan has a professional minor league system as well, though it is much smaller than the American version—each team has only one farm club in contrast to MLB teams' four or five.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RW_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-RW-80">[80]</a></sup></p>
<p>After World War II, professional leagues were founded in many Latin American nations, most prominently <a href="/wiki/Venezuela" title="Venezuela">Venezuela</a> (1946) and the Dominican Republic (1955).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> Since the early 1970s, the annual <a href="/wiki/Caribbean_Series" title="Caribbean Series">Caribbean Series</a> has matched the championship clubs from the four leading Latin American winter leagues: the <a href="/wiki/Dominican_Professional_Baseball_League" title="Dominican Professional Baseball League">Dominican Professional Baseball League</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mexican_Pacific_League" title="Mexican Pacific League">Mexican Pacific League</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Puerto_Rican_Professional_Baseball_League" title="Puerto Rican Professional Baseball League">Puerto Rican Professional Baseball League</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Venezuelan_Professional_Baseball_League" title="Venezuelan Professional Baseball League">Venezuelan Professional Baseball League</a>. In Asia, <a href="/wiki/Baseball_in_South_Korea" title="Baseball in South Korea">South Korea</a> (1982), <a href="/wiki/Professional_baseball_in_Taiwan" title="Professional baseball in Taiwan">Taiwan</a> (1990), and China (2003) all have professional leagues.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup></p>
<p>Many European countries have professional leagues as well, the most successful, other than the <a href="/wiki/Honkbal_Hoofdklasse" title="Honkbal Hoofdklasse">Dutch league</a>, being the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Serie_A1_(baseball)" title="Serie A1 (baseball)">Italian league</a> founded in 1948.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> Compared to those in Asia and Latin America, the various European leagues and the one in Australia historically have had no more than niche appeal. In 2004, Australia won a surprise silver medal at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2004_Olympic_Games" title="2004 Olympic Games">Olympic Games</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Israel_Baseball_League" title="Israel Baseball League">Israel Baseball League</a>, launched in 2007, folded after one season.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> The Confédération Européene de Baseball (European Baseball Confederation), founded in 1953, organizes a number of competitions between clubs from different countries, as well as national squads. Other competitions between national teams, such as the <a href="/wiki/Baseball_World_Cup" title="Baseball World Cup">Baseball World Cup</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Baseball_at_the_Summer_Olympics" title="Baseball at the Summer Olympics">Olympic baseball tournament</a>, were administered by the <a href="/wiki/International_Baseball_Federation" title="International Baseball Federation">International Baseball Federation</a> (IBAF) from its formation in 1938 until its 2013 merger with the <a href="/wiki/International_Softball_Federation" title="International Softball Federation">International Softball Federation</a> to create the current joint governing body for both sports, the <a href="/wiki/World_Baseball_Softball_Confederation" title="World Baseball Softball Confederation">World Baseball Softball Confederation</a> (WBSC). By 2009, the IBAF had 117 member countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_baseball" title="Women's baseball">Women's baseball</a> is played on an organized amateur basis in many of the countries where it is a leading men's sport. Since 2004, the IBAF and now WBSC have sanctioned the <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_Baseball_World_Cup" title="Women's Baseball World Cup">Women's Baseball World Cup</a>, featuring national teams.</p>
<p>After being admitted to the Olympics as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Olympic_sport" title="Olympic sport">medal sport</a> beginning with the <a href="/wiki/1992_Summer_Olympics" title="1992 Summer Olympics">1992 Games</a>, baseball was dropped from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2012_Summer_Olympic_Games" title="2012 Summer Olympic Games">2012 Summer Olympic Games</a> at the 2005 <a href="/wiki/International_Olympic_Committee" title="International Olympic Committee">International Olympic Committee</a> <a href="/wiki/117th_IOC_Session" title="117th IOC Session">meeting</a>. It remained part of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2008_Summer_Olympic_Games" title="2008 Summer Olympic Games">2008 Games</a>. The elimination of baseball, along with softball, from the 2012 Olympic program enabled the IOC to consider adding two different sports, but none received the votes required for inclusion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> While the sport's lack of a following in much of the world was a factor, more important was Major League Baseball's reluctance to have a break during the Games to allow its players to participate, as the <a href="/wiki/National_Hockey_League" title="National Hockey League">National Hockey League</a> now does during the <a href="/wiki/Winter_Olympic_Games" title="Winter Olympic Games">Winter Olympic Games</a>. Such a break is more difficult for MLB to accommodate because it would force the playoffs deeper into cold weather.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> Seeking reinstatement for the <a href="/wiki/2016_Summer_Olympics" title="2016 Summer Olympics">2016 Summer Olympics</a>, the IBAF proposed an abbreviated competition designed to facilitate the participation of top players, but the effort failed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> Major League Baseball initiated the <a href="/wiki/World_Baseball_Classic" title="World Baseball Classic">World Baseball Classic</a>, scheduled to precede the major league season, partly as a replacement, high-profile international tournament. The <a href="/wiki/2006_World_Baseball_Classic" title="2006 World Baseball Classic">inaugural Classic</a>, held in March 2006, was the first tournament involving national teams to feature a significant number of MLB participants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> The Baseball World Cup was discontinued after its <a href="/wiki/2011_Baseball_World_Cup" title="2011 Baseball World Cup">2011 edition</a> in favor of an expanded World Baseball Classic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Rules_and_gameplay">Rules and gameplay</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Baseball_rules" title="Baseball rules">Baseball rules</a></div>
<p>A game is played between two teams, each composed of nine players, that take turns playing offense (<a href="/wiki/Batting_(baseball)" title="Batting (baseball)">batting</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baserunning" title="Baserunning">baserunning</a>) and defense (<a href="/wiki/Pitch_(baseball)" title="Pitch (baseball)">pitching</a> and fielding). A pair of turns, one at bat and one in the field, by each team constitutes an <a href="/wiki/Inning" title="Inning">inning</a>. A game consists of nine innings (seven innings at the high school level and in <a href="/wiki/Doubleheader_(baseball)" title="Doubleheader (baseball)">doubleheaders</a> in college and minor leagues). One team—customarily the visiting team—bats in the top, or first half, of every inning. The other team—customarily the home team—bats in the bottom, or second half, of every inning. The goal of the game is to score more points (<a href="/wiki/Run_(baseball)" title="Run (baseball)">runs</a>) than the other team. The players on the team at bat attempt to score runs by circling or completing a tour of the four bases set at the corners of the square-shaped <a href="/wiki/Baseball_field" title="Baseball field">baseball diamond</a>. A player bats at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Home_plate" title="Home plate">home plate</a> and must proceed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Counterclockwise" title="Counterclockwise">counterclockwise</a> to first base, second base, third base, and back home in order to score a run. The team in the field attempts both to prevent runs from scoring and to record <a href="/wiki/Out_(baseball)" title="Out (baseball)">outs</a>, which remove opposing players from offensive action until their turn in their team's <a href="/wiki/Batting_order_(baseball)" title="Batting order (baseball)">batting order</a> comes up again. When three outs are recorded, the teams switch roles for the next half-inning. If the score of the game is tied after nine innings, <a href="/wiki/Extra_innings" title="Extra innings">extra innings</a> are played to resolve the contest. Many amateur games, particularly unorganized ones, involve different numbers of players and innings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Baseball_diamond.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="650" height="269" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Baseball_diamond.svg/350px-Baseball_diamond.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Baseball_diamond.svg/525px-Baseball_diamond.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Baseball_diamond.svg/700px-Baseball_diamond.svg.png 2x" width="350"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Baseball_diamond.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Diagram of a <a href="/wiki/Baseball_field" title="Baseball field">baseball field</a> (the term <i>diamond</i> may be used to refer to the square area defined by the four bases or to the entire playing field). The dimensions given are for professional and professional-style games. Children often play on smaller fields.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The game is played on a field whose primary boundaries, the foul lines, extend forward from home plate at 45-degree angles. The 90-degree area within the foul lines is referred to as fair territory; the 270-degree area outside them is foul territory. The part of the field enclosed by the bases and several yards beyond them is the <a href="/wiki/Infield" title="Infield">infield</a>; the area farther beyond the infield is the <a href="/wiki/Outfield" title="Outfield">outfield</a>. In the middle of the infield is a raised pitcher's mound, with a rectangular rubber plate (the rubber) at its center. The outer boundary of the outfield is typically demarcated by a raised fence, which may be of any material and height (many amateur games are played on unfenced fields). Fair territory between home plate and the outfield boundary is baseball's field of play, though significant events can take place in foul territory, as well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup></p>
<p>There are three basic tools of baseball: the <a href="/wiki/Baseball_(ball)" title="Baseball (ball)">ball</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Baseball_bat" title="Baseball bat">bat</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Baseball_glove" title="Baseball glove">glove or mitt</a>:</p>
<ul>
<li>The baseball is about the size of an adult's fist, around 9 inches (23 centimeters) in circumference. It has a rubber or cork center, wound in yarn and covered in white cowhide, with red stitching.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup></li>
<li>The bat is a hitting tool, traditionally made of a single, solid piece of wood. Other materials are now commonly used for nonprofessional games. It is a hard round stick, about 2.5 inches (6.4 centimeters) in diameter at the hitting end, tapering to a narrower handle and culminating in a knob. Bats used by adults are typically around 34 inches (86 centimeters) long, and not longer than 42 inches (106 centimeters).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup></li>
<li>The glove or mitt is a fielding tool, made of padded leather with webbing between the fingers. As an aid in catching and holding onto the ball, it takes various shapes to meet the specific needs of different fielding positions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<p>Protective helmets are also standard equipment for all batters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup></p>
<p>At the beginning of each half-inning, the nine players on the fielding team arrange themselves around the field. One of them, the <a href="/wiki/Pitcher" title="Pitcher">pitcher</a>, stands on the pitcher's mound. The pitcher begins the pitching delivery with one foot on the rubber, pushing off it to gain velocity when throwing toward home plate. Another player, the <a href="/wiki/Catcher" title="Catcher">catcher</a>, squats on the far side of home plate, facing the pitcher. The rest of the team faces home plate, typically arranged as four infielders—who set up along or within a few yards outside the imaginary lines between first, second, and third base—and three outfielders. In the <a href="/wiki/Baseball_positions" title="Baseball positions">standard arrangement</a>, there is a <a href="/wiki/First_baseman" title="First baseman">first baseman</a> positioned several steps to the left of first base, a <a href="/wiki/Second_baseman" title="Second baseman">second baseman</a> to the right of second base, a <a href="/wiki/Shortstop" title="Shortstop">shortstop</a> to the left of second base, and a <a href="/wiki/Third_baseman" title="Third baseman">third baseman</a> to the right of third base. The basic outfield positions are <a href="/wiki/Left_fielder" title="Left fielder">left fielder</a>, <a href="/wiki/Center_fielder" title="Center fielder">center fielder</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Right_fielder" title="Right fielder">right fielder</a>. A neutral <a href="/wiki/Umpire_(baseball)" title="Umpire (baseball)">umpire</a> sets up behind the catcher.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> Other umpires will be distributed around the field as well, though the number will vary depending on the level of play, amateur or children's games may only have an umpire behind the plate, while as many as six umpires can be used for important Major League Baseball games.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:David-ortiz-batters-box.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1450" data-file-width="2175" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/David-ortiz-batters-box.JPG/220px-David-ortiz-batters-box.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/David-ortiz-batters-box.JPG/330px-David-ortiz-batters-box.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/David-ortiz-batters-box.JPG/440px-David-ortiz-batters-box.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:David-ortiz-batters-box.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/David_Ortiz" title="David Ortiz">David Ortiz</a>, the batter, awaiting a pitch, with the catcher, and umpire</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Play starts with a batter standing at home plate, holding a bat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RulesPuttingBallInPlay_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-RulesPuttingBallInPlay-99">[99]</a></sup> The batter waits for the pitcher to throw a pitch (the ball) toward home plate, and attempts to hit the ball<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> with the bat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RulesPuttingBallInPlay_99-1"><a href="#cite_note-RulesPuttingBallInPlay-99">[99]</a></sup> The catcher catches pitches that the batter does not hit—as a result of either electing not to swing or failing to connect—and returns them to the pitcher. A batter who hits the ball into the field of play must drop the bat and begin running toward first base, at which point the player is referred to as a <i>runner</i> (or, until the play is over, a <i>batter-runner</i>). A batter-runner who reaches first base without being <a href="/wiki/Putout" title="Putout">put out</a> (see below) is said to be <i><a href="/wiki/Safe_(baseball)" title="Safe (baseball)">safe</a></i> and is now on base. A batter-runner may choose to remain at first base or attempt to advance to second base or even beyond—however far the player believes can be reached safely. A player who reaches base despite proper play by the fielders has recorded a <a href="/wiki/Hit_(baseball)" title="Hit (baseball)">hit</a>. A player who reaches first base safely on a hit is credited with a <a href="/wiki/Single_(baseball)" title="Single (baseball)">single</a>. If a player makes it to second base safely as a direct result of a hit, it is a <a href="/wiki/Double_(baseball)" title="Double (baseball)">double</a>; third base, a <a href="/wiki/Triple_(baseball)" title="Triple (baseball)">triple</a>. If the ball is hit in the air within the foul lines over the entire outfield (and outfield fence, if there is one), it is a <a href="/wiki/Home_run" title="Home run">home run</a>: the batter and any runners on base may all freely circle the bases, each scoring a run. This is the most desirable result for the batter. A player who reaches base due to a fielding mistake is not credited with a hit—instead, the responsible fielder is charged with an <a href="/wiki/Error_(baseball)" title="Error (baseball)">error</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RulesPuttingBallInPlay_99-2"><a href="#cite_note-RulesPuttingBallInPlay-99">[99]</a></sup></p>
<p>Any runners already on base may attempt to advance on batted balls that land, or contact the ground, in fair territory, before or after the ball lands. A runner on first base <i>must</i> attempt to advance if a ball lands in play. If a ball hit into play rolls foul before passing through the infield, it becomes <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dead_ball_(baseball)" title="Dead ball (baseball)">dead</a> and any runners must return to the base they were at when the play began. If the ball is hit in the air and caught before it lands, the batter has <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flyout" title="Flyout">flied out</a> and any runners on base may attempt to advance only if they <a href="/wiki/Tag_up" title="Tag up">tag up</a> or touch the base they were at when the play began, as or after the ball is caught. Runners may also attempt to advance to the next base while the pitcher is in the process of delivering the ball to home plate—a successful effort is a <a href="/wiki/Stolen_base" title="Stolen base">stolen base</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup></p>
<p>A pitch that is not hit into the field of play is called either a strike or a ball. A batter against whom three strikes are recorded <a href="/wiki/Strikeout" title="Strikeout">strikes out</a>. A batter against whom four balls are recorded is awarded a <a href="/wiki/Base_on_balls" title="Base on balls">base on balls</a> or walk, a free advance to first base. (A batter may also freely advance to first base if the batter's body or uniform is struck by a pitch outside the strike zone, provided the batter does not swing and attempts to avoid being hit.)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> Crucial to determining balls and strikes is the umpire's judgment as to whether a pitch has passed through the <a href="/wiki/Strike_zone" title="Strike zone">strike zone</a>, a conceptual area above home plate extending from the midpoint between the batter's shoulders and belt down to the hollow of the knee.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MLBR2_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-MLBR2-103">[103]</a></sup></p>
<p>A strike is called when one of the following happens:</p>
<ul>
<li>The batter lets a well-pitched ball (one within the strike zone) go through to the catcher.</li>
<li>The batter swings at any ball (even one outside the strike zone) and misses, or <a href="/wiki/Foul_tip" title="Foul tip">foul tips</a> it directly into the catcher's hands.</li>
<li>The batter hits a <a href="/wiki/Foul_ball" title="Foul ball">foul ball</a>—one that either initially lands in foul territory or initially lands within the diamond but moves into foul territory before passing first or third base. If there are already two strikes on the batter, a foul ball is not counted as a third strike; thus, a foul ball cannot result in the immediate strikeout of the batter. (There is an exception to this exception: a two-strike foul <a href="/wiki/Bunt_(baseball)" title="Bunt (baseball)">bunt</a> is recorded as a third strike.)</li>
</ul>
<p>A ball is called when the pitcher throws a pitch that is outside the strike zone, provided the batter has not swung at it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MLBR2_103-1"><a href="#cite_note-MLBR2-103">[103]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Navy_baseball.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1911" data-file-width="3000" height="140" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Navy_baseball.jpg/220px-Navy_baseball.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Navy_baseball.jpg/330px-Navy_baseball.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Navy_baseball.jpg/440px-Navy_baseball.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Navy_baseball.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A <a href="/wiki/Shortstop" title="Shortstop">shortstop</a> tries to <a href="/wiki/Tag_out" title="Tag out">tag out</a> a runner who is sliding headfirst, attempting to reach second base.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>While the team at bat is trying to score runs, the team in the field is attempting to record outs. Among the various ways a member of the batting team may be put out, five are most common:</p>
<ul>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Strikeout" title="Strikeout">strikeout</a>: as described above, recorded against a batter who makes three strikes before putting the ball into play or being awarded a free advance to first base (see also <a href="/wiki/Uncaught_third_strike" title="Uncaught third strike">uncaught third strike</a>).</li>
<li>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flyout" title="Flyout">flyout</a>: as described above, recorded against a batter who hits a ball in the air that is caught by a fielder, whether in fair territory or foul territory, before it lands, whether or not the batter has run.</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Force_play" title="Force play">ground out</a>: recorded against a batter (in this case, batter-runner) who hits a ball that lands in fair territory which, before the batter-runner can reach first base, is retrieved by a fielder who touches first base while holding the ball or relays it to another fielder who touches first base while holding the ball.</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Force_play" title="Force play">force out</a>: recorded against a runner who is required to attempt to advance—either because the runner is on first base and a batted ball lands in fair territory, or because the runner immediately behind on the basepath is thus required to attempt to advance—but fails to reach the next base before a fielder touches the base while holding the ball. The ground out is technically a special case of the force out.</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Tag_out" title="Tag out">tag out</a>: recorded against a runner who is touched by a fielder with the ball or a glove holding the ball, while the runner is not touching a base.</li>
</ul>
<p>It is possible to record two outs in the course of the same play—a <a href="/wiki/Double_play" title="Double play">double play</a>. Even three—a <a href="/wiki/Triple_play" title="Triple play">triple play</a>—is possible, though this is very rare. Players put out or retired must leave the field, returning to their team's <a href="/wiki/Dugout_(baseball)" title="Dugout (baseball)">dugout</a> or bench. A runner may be stranded on base when a third out is recorded against another player on the team. Stranded runners do not benefit the team in its next turn at bat—every half-inning begins with the bases empty of runners.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup></p>
<p>An individual player's turn batting or <a href="/wiki/Plate_appearance" title="Plate appearance">plate appearance</a> is complete when the player reaches base, hits a home run, makes an out, or hits a ball that results in the team's third out, even if it is recorded against a teammate. On rare occasions, a batter may be at the plate when, without the batter's hitting the ball, a third out is recorded against a teammate—for instance, a runner getting <a href="/wiki/Caught_stealing" title="Caught stealing">caught stealing</a> (tagged out attempting to steal a base). A batter with this sort of incomplete plate appearance starts off the team's next turn batting; any balls or strikes recorded against the batter the previous inning are erased. A runner may circle the bases only once per plate appearance and thus can score at most a single run per batting turn. Once a player has completed a plate appearance, that player may not bat again until the eight other members of the player's team have all taken their turn at bat. The batting order is set before the game begins, and may not be altered except for substitutions. Once a player has been removed for a substitute, that player may not reenter the game. Children's games often have more liberal substitution rules.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup></p>
<p>If the <a href="/wiki/Designated_hitter" title="Designated hitter">designated hitter</a> (DH) rule is in effect, each team has a tenth player whose sole responsibility is to bat (and run). The DH takes the place of another player—almost invariably the pitcher—in the batting order, but does not field.
