Thus, even with the DH, each team still has a batting order of nine players and a fielding arrangement of nine players.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Personnel">Personnel</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Baseball_positions" title="Baseball positions">Baseball positions</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Player_rosters">Player rosters</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Baseball_positions.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="906" data-file-width="998" height="272" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Baseball_positions.svg/300px-Baseball_positions.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Baseball_positions.svg/450px-Baseball_positions.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Baseball_positions.svg/600px-Baseball_positions.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Baseball_positions.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Defensive positions on a baseball field, with abbreviations and scorekeeper's position numbers (not uniform numbers).</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Roster, or squad, sizes differ between different leagues and different levels of organized play. Major League Baseball teams maintain 25-player active rosters. A typical 25-man roster in a league without the DH rule, such as MLB's National League, features:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li>eight <a href="/wiki/Position_player" title="Position player">position players</a>—catcher, four infielders, three outfielders—who play on a regular basis</li>
<li>five <a href="/wiki/Starting_pitcher" title="Starting pitcher">starting pitchers</a> who constitute the team's pitching rotation or starting rotation</li>
<li>six <a href="/wiki/Relief_pitcher" title="Relief pitcher">relief pitchers</a>, including one specialist <a href="/wiki/Closer_(baseball)" title="Closer (baseball)">closer</a>, who constitute the team's <a href="/wiki/Bullpen" title="Bullpen">bullpen</a> (named for the off-field area where pitchers warm up)</li>
<li>one backup, or substitute, catcher</li>
<li>two backup infielders</li>
<li>two backup outfielders</li>
<li>one specialist <a href="/wiki/Pinch_hitter" title="Pinch hitter">pinch hitter</a>, or a second backup catcher, or a seventh reliever</li>
</ul>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/American_League" title="American League">American League</a> and others with the DH rule, there will usually be nine offensive regulars (including the DH), five starting pitchers, seven or eight relievers, a backup catcher and two or three other reserves; the need for late inning pinch-hitters (usually in the pitcher's spot) is reduced by the DH.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_personnel">Other personnel</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jacob%27s_Field_Fultz_and_Betancourt_in_the_Bullpen.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1280" data-file-width="960" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Jacob%27s_Field_Fultz_and_Betancourt_in_the_Bullpen.jpg/220px-Jacob%27s_Field_Fultz_and_Betancourt_in_the_Bullpen.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Jacob%27s_Field_Fultz_and_Betancourt_in_the_Bullpen.jpg/330px-Jacob%27s_Field_Fultz_and_Betancourt_in_the_Bullpen.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Jacob%27s_Field_Fultz_and_Betancourt_in_the_Bullpen.jpg/440px-Jacob%27s_Field_Fultz_and_Betancourt_in_the_Bullpen.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jacob%27s_Field_Fultz_and_Betancourt_in_the_Bullpen.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Relief_pitcher" title="Relief pitcher">Relief pitchers</a> warming up, overseen by a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pitching_coach" title="Pitching coach">bullpen coach</a>. A <a href="/wiki/Manager_(baseball)" title="Manager (baseball)">manager</a> will often have both a right-handed and a left-handed reliever warm up to maximize strategic options.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Manager_(baseball)" title="Manager (baseball)">manager</a>, or head coach of a team, oversees the team's major strategic decisions, such as establishing the starting rotation, setting the lineup, or batting order, before each game, and making substitutions during games—in particular, bringing in relief pitchers. Managers are typically assisted by two or more <a href="/wiki/Coach_(baseball)" title="Coach (baseball)">coaches</a>; they may have specialized responsibilities, such as working with players on hitting, fielding, pitching, or strength and conditioning. At most levels of organized play, two coaches are stationed on the field when the team is at bat: the first base coach and third base coach, occupying designated coaches' boxes just outside the foul lines, assist in the direction of baserunners when the ball is in play, and relay tactical signals from the manager to batters and runners during pauses in play.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> In contrast to many other team sports, baseball managers and coaches generally wear their team's uniforms; coaches must be in uniform in order to be allowed on the playing field during a game.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup></p>
<p>Any baseball game involves one or more <a href="/wiki/Umpire_(baseball)" title="Umpire (baseball)">umpires</a>, who make rulings on the outcome of each play. At a minimum, one umpire will stand behind the catcher, to have a good view of the strike zone, and call balls and strikes. Additional umpires may be stationed near the other bases, thus making it easier to judge plays such as attempted force outs and tag outs. In Major League Baseball, four umpires are used for each game, one near each base. In the playoffs, six umpires are used: one at each base and two in the outfield along the foul lines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Strategy_and_tactics">Strategy and tactics</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
<div class="PopUpMediaTransform" id="mwe_player_0" style="width:220px;" videopayload='&lt;div class="mediaContainer" style="width:854px"&gt;&lt;video id="mwe_player_1" poster="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Matthew_Dipasupil_Summer_2014_Baseball_Video.webm/854px--Matthew_Dipasupil_Summer_2014_Baseball_Video.webm.jpg" controls="" preload="none" autoplay="" style="width:854px;height:480px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="746.215" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="Matthew_Dipasupil_Summer_2014_Baseball_Video.webm" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/b/b8/Matthew_Dipasupil_Summer_2014_Baseball_Video.webm/Matthew_Dipasupil_Summer_2014_Baseball_Video.webm.480p.webm" type="video/webm; <div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Matthew_Dipasupil_Summer_2014_Baseball_Video.webm" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Matthew Dipasupil Summer 2014 Baseball Video</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Baseball_positioning" title="Baseball positioning">Baseball positioning</a></div>
<p>Many of the pre-game and in-game strategic decisions in baseball revolve around a fundamental fact: in general, right-handed batters tend to be more successful against left-handed pitchers and, to an even greater degree, left-handed batters tend to be more successful against right-handed pitchers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> A manager with several left-handed batters in the regular lineup who knows the team will be facing a left-handed starting pitcher may respond by starting one or more of the right-handed backups on the team's roster. During the late innings of a game, as relief pitchers and pinch hitters are brought in, the opposing managers will often go back and forth trying to create favorable matchups with their substitutions: the manager of the fielding team trying to arrange same-handed pitcher-batter matchups, the manager of the batting team trying to arrange opposite-handed matchups. With a team that has the lead in the late innings, a manager may remove a starting position player—especially one whose turn at bat is not likely to come up again—for a more skillful fielder.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pitching_and_fielding_tactics">Pitching and fielding tactics</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Baseball_pick-off_attempt.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="391" data-file-width="600" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Baseball_pick-off_attempt.jpg/220px-Baseball_pick-off_attempt.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Baseball_pick-off_attempt.jpg/330px-Baseball_pick-off_attempt.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Baseball_pick-off_attempt.jpg/440px-Baseball_pick-off_attempt.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Baseball_pick-off_attempt.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A <a href="/wiki/First_baseman" title="First baseman">first baseman</a> receives a <a href="/wiki/Pickoff" title="Pickoff">pickoff</a> throw, as the runner dives back to first base.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The tactical decision that precedes almost every play in a baseball game involves pitch selection. By gripping and then releasing the baseball in a certain manner, and by throwing it at a certain speed, pitchers can cause the baseball to break to either side, or downward, as it approaches the batter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-www.baseballexplained.com_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-www.baseballexplained.com-114">[114]</a></sup> Among the resulting wide variety of pitches that may be thrown, the four basic types are the <a href="/wiki/Fastball" title="Fastball">fastball</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Changeup" title="Changeup">changeup</a> (or off-speed pitch), and two <a href="/wiki/Breaking_ball" title="Breaking ball">breaking balls</a>—the <a href="/wiki/Curveball" title="Curveball">curveball</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Slider" title="Slider">slider</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> Pitchers have different repertoires of pitches they are skillful at throwing. Conventionally, before each pitch, the catcher signals the pitcher what type of pitch to throw, as well as its general vertical and/or horizontal location.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> If there is disagreement on the selection, the pitcher may <a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_baseball_(S)#shake_off" title="Glossary of baseball (S)">shake off the sign</a> and the catcher will call for a different pitch. With a runner on base and <a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_baseball_(L)#lead" title="Glossary of baseball (L)">taking a lead</a>, the pitcher may attempt a <a href="/wiki/Pickoff" title="Pickoff">pickoff</a>, a quick throw to a fielder <a href="/wiki/Covering_a_base" title="Covering a base">covering the base</a> to keep the runner's lead in check or, optimally, effect a tag out. Pickoff attempts, however, are subject to rules that severely restrict the pitcher's movements before and during the pickoff attempt. Violation of any one of these rules could result in the umpire calling a <a href="/wiki/Balk" title="Balk">balk</a> against the pitcher, with the result being runners on base, if any, advance one base with impunity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-www.baseballexplained.com_114-1"><a href="#cite_note-www.baseballexplained.com-114">[114]</a></sup> If an attempted stolen base is anticipated, the catcher may call for a <a href="/wiki/Pitchout" title="Pitchout">pitchout</a>, a ball thrown deliberately off the plate, allowing the catcher to catch it while standing and throw quickly to a base.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> Facing a batter with a strong tendency to hit to one side of the field, the fielding team may employ a <a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_baseball_(S)#shift" title="Glossary of baseball (S)">shift</a>, with most or all of the fielders moving to the left or right of their usual positions. With a runner on third base, the infielders may <a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_baseball_(P)#playing_in" title="Glossary of baseball (P)">play in</a>, moving closer to home plate to improve the odds of throwing out the runner on a <a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_baseball_(G)#ground_ball" title="Glossary of baseball (G)">ground ball</a>, though a sharply hit grounder is more likely to carry through a drawn-in infield.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SB45_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-SB45-118">[118]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Batting_and_baserunning_tactics">Batting and baserunning tactics</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_bunt.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1930" data-file-width="2897" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/The_bunt.jpg/220px-The_bunt.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/The_bunt.jpg/330px-The_bunt.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/The_bunt.jpg/440px-The_bunt.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_bunt.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A batter squares to bunt, moving his hands up the barrel of the bat to increase his control and deaden the ball on impact.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Several basic offensive tactics come into play with a runner on first base, including the fundamental choice of whether to attempt a steal of second base. The <a href="/wiki/Hit_and_run_(baseball)" title="Hit and run (baseball)">hit and run</a> is sometimes employed with a skillful <a href="/wiki/Contact_hitter" title="Contact hitter">contact hitter</a>: the runner takes off with the pitch drawing the shortstop or second baseman over to second base, creating a gap in the infield for the batter to poke the ball through.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Sacrifice_bunt" title="Sacrifice bunt">sacrifice bunt</a> calls for the batter to focus on making contact with the ball so that it rolls a short distance into the infield, allowing the runner to advance into <a href="/wiki/Scoring_position" title="Scoring position">scoring position</a> even at the expense of the batter being thrown out at first—a batter who succeeds is credited with a sacrifice. (A batter, particularly one who is a fast runner, may also attempt to <a href="/wiki/Bunt_(baseball)" title="Bunt (baseball)">bunt</a> for a hit.) A sacrifice bunt employed with a runner on third base, aimed at bringing that runner home, is known as a <a href="/wiki/Squeeze_play_(baseball)" title="Squeeze play (baseball)">squeeze play</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> With a runner on third and fewer than two outs, a batter may instead concentrate on hitting a fly ball that, even if it is caught, will be deep enough to allow the runner to tag up and score—a successful batter in this case gets credit for a <a href="/wiki/Sacrifice_fly" title="Sacrifice fly">sacrifice fly</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SB45_118-1"><a href="#cite_note-SB45-118">[118]</a></sup> The manager will sometimes signal a batter who is <a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_baseball_(A)#ahead_in_the_count" title="Glossary of baseball (A)">ahead in the count</a> (i.e., has more balls than strikes) to <a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_baseball_(T)#take_sign" title="Glossary of baseball (T)">take</a>, or not swing at, the next pitch.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Distinctive_elements">Distinctive elements</span></h2>
<p>Baseball has certain attributes that set it apart from the other popular team sports in the countries where it has a following, including <a href="/wiki/American_football" title="American football">American</a> and <a href="/wiki/Canadian_football" title="Canadian football">Canadian football</a>, <a href="/wiki/Basketball" title="Basketball">basketball</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ice_hockey" title="Ice hockey">ice hockey</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Association_football" title="Association football">soccer</a>. All of these sports use a clock; in all of them, play is less individual and more collective; and in none of them is the variation between playing fields nearly as substantial or important. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Comparison_between_cricket_and_baseball" title="Comparison between cricket and baseball">comparison between cricket and baseball</a> demonstrates that many of baseball's distinctive elements are shared in various ways with its cousin sports.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="No_clock_to_kill">No clock to kill</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:A_worn-out_baseball.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2405" data-file-width="2900" height="182" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/A_worn-out_baseball.JPG/220px-A_worn-out_baseball.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/A_worn-out_baseball.JPG/330px-A_worn-out_baseball.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/A_worn-out_baseball.JPG/440px-A_worn-out_baseball.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:A_worn-out_baseball.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A well-worn baseball</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In clock-limited sports, games often end with a team that holds the lead <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stalling_(gaming)" title="Stalling (gaming)">killing the clock</a> rather than competing aggressively against the opposing team. In contrast, baseball has no clock; a team cannot win without getting the last batter out and rallies are not constrained by time. At almost any turn in any baseball game, the most advantageous strategy is some form of aggressive strategy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> In contrast, again, the clock comes into play even in the case of multi-day <a href="/wiki/Test_cricket" title="Test cricket">Test</a> and <a href="/wiki/First-class_cricket" title="First-class cricket">first-class cricket</a>: the possibility of a draw often encourages a team that is batting last and well behind to bat defensively, giving up any faint chance at a win to avoid a loss.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup> Baseball offers no such reward for conservative batting.</p>
<p>While nine innings has been the standard since the beginning of professional baseball, the duration of the average major league game has increased steadily through the years. At the turn of the 20th century, games typically took an hour and a half to play. In the 1920s, they averaged just less than two hours, which eventually ballooned to 2:38 in 1960.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HB_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-HB-124">[124]</a></sup> By 1997, the average American League game lasted 2:57 (National League games were about 10 minutes shorter—pitchers at the plate making for quicker outs than designated hitters).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JG_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-JG-125">[125]</a></sup> In 2004, Major League Baseball declared that its goal was an average game of merely 2:45.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HB_124-1"><a href="#cite_note-HB-124">[124]</a></sup> By 2014, though, the average MLB game took over three hours to complete.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> The lengthening of games is attributed to longer breaks between half-innings for television commercials, increased offense, more pitching changes, and a slower pace of play with pitchers taking more time between each delivery, and batters stepping out of the box more frequently.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HB_124-2"><a href="#cite_note-HB-124">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JG_125-1"><a href="#cite_note-JG-125">[125]</a></sup> Other leagues have experienced similar issues. In 2008, <a href="/wiki/Nippon_Professional_Baseball" title="Nippon Professional Baseball">Nippon Professional Baseball</a> took steps aimed at shortening games by 12 minutes from the preceding decade's average of 3:18.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Individual_focus">Individual focus</span></h3>
<p>Although baseball is a team sport, individual players are often placed under scrutiny and pressure. In 1915, a baseball instructional manual pointed out that every single pitch, of which there are often more than two hundred in a game, involves an individual, one-on-one contest: "the pitcher and the batter in a battle of wits".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup> Contrasting the game with both football and basketball, scholar Michael Mandelbaum argues that "baseball is the one closest in evolutionary descent to the older individual sports".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> Pitcher, batter, and fielder all act essentially independent of each other. While coaching staffs can signal pitcher or batter to pursue certain tactics, the execution of the play itself is a series of solitary acts. If the batter hits a line drive, the outfielder is solely responsible for deciding to try to catch it or play it on the bounce and for succeeding or failing. The statistical precision of baseball is both facilitated by this isolation and reinforces it. As described by Mandelbaum,</p>
<blockquote>
<p>It is impossible to isolate and objectively assess the contribution each [football] team member makes to the outcome of the play ... [E]very basketball player is interacting with all of his teammates all the time. In baseball, by contrast, every player is more or less on his own ... Baseball is therefore a realm of complete transparency and total responsibility. A baseball player lives in a glass house, and in a stark moral universe ... Everything that every player does is accounted for and everything accounted for is either good or bad, right or wrong.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Cricket is more similar to baseball than many other team sports in this regard: while the individual focus in cricket is mitigated by the importance of the <a href="/wiki/Partnership_(cricket)" title="Partnership (cricket)">batting partnership</a> and the practicalities of tandem running, it is enhanced by the fact that a batsman may occupy the <a href="/wiki/Wicket" title="Wicket">wicket</a> for an hour or much more. There is no statistical equivalent in cricket for the fielding error and thus less emphasis on personal responsibility in this area of play.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Uniqueness_of_each_baseball_park">Uniqueness of each baseball park</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Baseball_park" title="Baseball park">Baseball park</a></div>
<p>Unlike those of most sports, baseball playing fields can vary significantly in size and shape. While the dimensions of the infield are specifically regulated, the only constraint on outfield size and shape for professional teams following the rules of Major League and Minor League Baseball is that fields built or remodeled since June 1, 1958, must have a minimum distance of 325 feet (99 m) from home plate to the fences in left and right field and 400 feet (122 m) to center.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup> Major league teams often skirt even this rule. For example, at <a href="/wiki/Minute_Maid_Park" title="Minute Maid Park">Minute Maid Park</a>, which became the home of the <a href="/wiki/Houston_Astros" title="Houston Astros">Houston Astros</a> in 2000, the <a href="/wiki/Crawford_Boxes" title="Crawford Boxes">Crawford Boxes</a> in left field are only 315 feet (96 m) from home plate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup> There are no rules at all that address the height of fences or other structures at the edge of the outfield. The most famously idiosyncratic outfield boundary is the left-field wall at Boston's <a href="/wiki/Fenway_Park" title="Fenway Park">Fenway Park</a>, in use since 1912: the <a href="/wiki/Green_Monster" title="Green Monster">Green Monster</a> is 310 feet (94 m) from home plate down the line and 37 feet (11 m) tall.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fenway_from_Legend%27s_Box.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1063" data-file-width="1600" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Fenway_from_Legend%27s_Box.jpg/220px-Fenway_from_Legend%27s_Box.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Fenway_from_Legend%27s_Box.jpg/330px-Fenway_from_Legend%27s_Box.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Fenway_from_Legend%27s_Box.jpg/440px-Fenway_from_Legend%27s_Box.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fenway_from_Legend%27s_Box.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Fenway_Park" title="Fenway Park">Fenway Park</a>, home of the <a href="/wiki/Boston_Red_Sox" title="Boston Red Sox">Boston Red Sox</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Green_Monster" title="Green Monster">Green Monster</a> is visible beyond the playing field on the left.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Similarly, there are no regulations at all concerning the dimensions of foul territory. Thus a foul fly ball may be entirely out of play in a park with little space between the foul lines and the stands, but a foulout in a park with more expansive foul ground.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup> A fence in foul territory that is close to the outfield line will tend to direct balls that strike it back toward the fielders, while one that is farther away may actually prompt more collisions, as outfielders run full speed to field balls deep in the corner. These variations can make the difference between a double and a triple or <a href="/wiki/Inside-the-park_home_run" title="Inside-the-park home run">inside-the-park home run</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup> The surface of the field is also unregulated. While the adjacent image shows a traditional field surfacing arrangement (and the one used by virtually all MLB teams with naturally surfaced fields), teams are free to decide what areas will be grassed or bare.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup> Some fields—including several in MLB—use an artificial surface, such as <a href="/wiki/AstroTurf" title="AstroTurf">AstroTurf</a>. Surface variations can have a significant effect on how ground balls behave and are fielded as well as on baserunning. Similarly, the presence of a roof (seven major league teams play in stadiums with permanent or retractable roofs) can greatly affect how fly balls are played.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup> While football and soccer players deal with similar variations of field surface and stadium covering, the size and shape of their fields are much more standardized. The area out-of-bounds on a football or soccer field does not affect play the way foul territory in baseball does, so variations in that regard are largely insignificant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup></p>
<p>These physical variations create a distinctive set of playing conditions at each ballpark. Other local factors, such as altitude and climate, can also significantly affect play. A given stadium may acquire a reputation as a pitcher's park or a hitter's park, if one or the other discipline notably benefits from its unique mix of elements. The most exceptional park in this regard is <a href="/wiki/Coors_Field" title="Coors Field">Coors Field</a>, home of the <a href="/wiki/Colorado_Rockies" title="Colorado Rockies">Colorado Rockies</a>. Its high altitude—5,282 feet (1,610 m) above sea level—is responsible for giving it the strongest hitter's park effect in the major leagues.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Wrigley_Field" title="Wrigley Field">Wrigley Field</a>, home of the <a href="/wiki/Chicago_Cubs" title="Chicago Cubs">Chicago Cubs</a>, is known for its fickle disposition: a hitter's park when the strong winds off <a href="/wiki/Lake_Michigan" title="Lake Michigan">Lake Michigan</a> are blowing out, it becomes more of a pitcher's park when they are blowing in.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup> The absence of a standardized field affects not only how particular games play out, but the nature of team rosters and players' statistical records. For example, hitting a fly ball 330 feet (100 m) into right field might result in an easy catch on the <a href="/wiki/Warning_track" title="Warning track">warning track</a> at one park, and a home run at another. A team that plays in a park with a relatively short right field, such as the New York Yankees, will tend to stock its roster with left-handed <a href="/wiki/Pull_hitter" title="Pull hitter">pull hitters</a>, who can best exploit it. On the individual level, a player who spends most of his career with a team that plays in a hitter's park will gain an advantage in batting statistics over time—even more so if his talents are especially suited to the park.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Statistics">Statistics</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Baseball_statistics" title="Baseball statistics">Baseball statistics</a></div>
<p>Organized baseball lends itself to <a href="/wiki/Statistics" title="Statistics">statistics</a> to a greater degree than many other sports. Each play is discrete and has a relatively small number of possible outcomes. In the late 19th century, a former cricket player, English-born <a href="/wiki/Henry_Chadwick_(writer)" title="Henry Chadwick (writer)">Henry Chadwick</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brooklyn,_New_York" title="Brooklyn, New York">Brooklyn, New York</a>, was responsible for the "development of the <a href="/wiki/Box_score_(baseball)" title="Box score (baseball)">box score</a>, tabular standings, the annual baseball guide, the <a href="/wiki/Batting_average" title="Batting average">batting average</a>, and most of the common statistics and tables used to describe baseball."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-T16_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-T16-143">[143]</a></sup> The statistical record is so central to the game's "historical essence" that Chadwick came to be known as Father Baseball.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-T16_143-1"><a href="#cite_note-T16-143">[143]</a></sup> In the 1920s, American newspapers began devoting more and more attention to baseball statistics, initiating what journalist and historian <a href="/wiki/Alan_Schwarz" title="Alan Schwarz">Alan Schwarz</a> describes as a "tectonic shift in sports, as intrigue that once focused mostly on teams began to go to individual players and their statistics lines."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Official Baseball Rules administered by Major League Baseball require the <a href="/wiki/Official_scorer" title="Official scorer">official scorer</a> to categorize each baseball play unambiguously. The rules provide detailed criteria to promote consistency. The <a href="/wiki/Baseball_scorekeeping" title="Baseball scorekeeping">score report</a> is the official basis for both the box score of the game and the relevant statistical records.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup> General managers, managers, and <a href="/wiki/Scout_(sport)" title="Scout (sport)">baseball scouts</a> use statistics to evaluate players and make strategic decisions.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Baseball_steal.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="466" data-file-width="720" height="142" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Baseball_steal.jpg/220px-Baseball_steal.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Baseball_steal.jpg/330px-Baseball_steal.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Baseball_steal.jpg/440px-Baseball_steal.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Baseball_steal.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Rickey_Henderson" title="Rickey Henderson">Rickey Henderson</a>—the major leagues' all-time leader in runs and stolen bases—stealing third base in a 1988 game.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Certain traditional statistics are familiar to most baseball fans. The basic batting statistics include:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/At_bats" title="At bats">At bats</a>: plate appearances, excluding walks and hit by pitches—where the batter's ability is not fully tested—and sacrifices and sacrifice flies—where the batter intentionally makes an out in order to advance one or more baserunners</li>
<li>Hits: times reached base because of a batted, fair ball without fielding error or <a href="/wiki/Fielder%27s_choice" title="Fielder's choice">fielder's choice</a></li>
<li>Runs: times circling the bases and reaching home safely</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Run_batted_in" title="Run batted in">Runs batted in</a> (RBIs): number of runners who scored due to a batter's action (including the batter, in the case of a home run), except when batter grounded into double play or reached on an error</li>
<li>Home runs: hits on which the batter successfully touched all four bases, without the contribution of a fielding error</li>
<li>Batting average: hits divided by at bats—the traditional measure of batting ability</li>
</ul>
<p>The basic baserunning statistics include:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li>Stolen bases: times advancing to the next base entirely due to the runner's own efforts, generally while the pitcher is preparing to deliver or delivering the ball</li>
<li>Caught stealing: times tagged out while attempting to steal a base</li>
</ul>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cy_young_pitching.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2404" data-file-width="2756" height="192" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Cy_young_pitching.jpg/220px-Cy_young_pitching.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Cy_young_pitching.jpg/330px-Cy_young_pitching.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Cy_young_pitching.jpg/440px-Cy_young_pitching.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cy_young_pitching.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Cy_Young" title="Cy Young">Cy Young</a>—the holder of many major league career marks, including wins and innings pitched, as well as losses—in 1908. MLB's annual <a href="/wiki/Cy_Young_Award" title="Cy Young Award">awards for the best pitcher in each league</a> are named for Young.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The basic pitching statistics include:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Win_(baseball)" title="Win (baseball)">Wins</a>: credited to pitcher on winning team who last pitched before the team took a lead that it never relinquished (a starting pitcher must pitch at least five innings to qualify for a win)</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Loss_(baseball)" title="Loss (baseball)">Losses</a>: charged to pitcher on losing team who was pitching when the opposing team took a lead that it never relinquished</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Save_(baseball)" title="Save (baseball)">Saves</a>: games where the pitcher enters a game led by the pitcher's team, finishes the game without surrendering the lead, is not the winning pitcher, and either (a) the lead was three runs or less when the pitcher entered the game; (b) the potential tying run was on base, at bat, or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/On_deck_(baseball)" title="On deck (baseball)">on deck</a>; or (c) the pitcher pitched three or more innings</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Innings_pitched" title="Innings pitched">Innings pitched</a>: outs recorded while pitching divided by three (partial innings are conventionally recorded as, e.g., "5.2" or "7.1", the last digit actually representing thirds, not tenths, of an inning)</li>
<li>Strikeouts: times pitching three strikes to a batter</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Winning_percentage" title="Winning percentage">Winning percentage</a>: wins divided by decisions (wins plus losses)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Earned_run_average" title="Earned run average">Earned run average</a> (ERA): runs allowed, excluding those resulting from fielding errors, per nine innings pitched</li>
</ul>
<p>The basic fielding statistics include:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Putout" title="Putout">Putouts</a>: times the fielder catches a fly ball, tags or forces out a runner, or otherwise directly effects an out</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Assist_(baseball)" title="Assist (baseball)">Assists</a>: times a putout by another fielder was recorded following the fielder touching the ball</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Error_(baseball)" title="Error (baseball)">Errors</a>: times the fielder fails to make a play that should have been made with common effort, and the batting team benefits as a result</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Total_chances" title="Total chances">Total chances</a>: putouts plus assists plus errors</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fielding_average" title="Fielding average">Fielding average</a>: successful chances (putouts plus assists) divided by total chances</li>
</ul>
<p>Among the many other statistics that are kept are those collectively known as <i>situational statistics</i>. For example, statistics can indicate which specific pitchers a certain batter performs best against. If a given situation statistically favors a certain batter, the manager of the fielding team may be more likely to change pitchers or have the pitcher <a href="/wiki/Intentional_base_on_balls" title="Intentional base on balls">intentionally walk</a> the batter in order to face one who is less likely to succeed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sabermetrics">Sabermetrics</span></h3>
<p><i><a href="/wiki/Sabermetrics" title="Sabermetrics">Sabermetrics</a></i> refers to the field of baseball statistical study and the development of new statistics and analytical tools. The term is also used to refer directly to new statistics themselves. The term was coined around 1980 by one of the field's leading proponents, <a href="/wiki/Bill_James" title="Bill James">Bill James</a>, and derives from the <a href="/wiki/Society_for_American_Baseball_Research" title="Society for American Baseball Research">Society for American Baseball Research</a> (SABR).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup></p>
<p>The growing popularity of sabermetrics since the early 1980s has brought more attention to two batting statistics that sabermetricians argue are much better gauges of a batter's skill than batting average:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/On-base_percentage" title="On-base percentage">On-base percentage</a> measures a batter's ability to get on base. It is calculated by taking the sum of the batter's successes in getting on base (hits plus walks plus hit by pitches) and dividing that by the batter's total plate appearances (at bats plus walks plus hit by pitches plus sacrifice flies), except for sacrifice bunts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Slugging_percentage" title="Slugging percentage">Slugging percentage</a> measures a batter's ability to hit for power. It is calculated by taking the batter's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Total_bases" title="Total bases">total bases</a> (one per each single, two per double, three per triple, and four per home run) and dividing that by the batter's at bats.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<p>Some of the new statistics devised by sabermetricians have gained wide use:</p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/On-base_plus_slugging" title="On-base plus slugging">On-base plus slugging</a> (OPS) measures a batter's overall ability. It is calculated by adding the batter's on-base percentage and slugging percentage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Walks_plus_hits_per_inning_pitched" title="Walks plus hits per inning pitched">Walks plus hits per inning pitched</a> (WHIP) measures a pitcher's ability at preventing hitters from reaching base. It is calculated exactly as its name suggests.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Popularity_and_cultural_impact">Popularity and cultural impact</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Waseda_University_baseball_players.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="944" data-file-width="664" height="242" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Waseda_University_baseball_players.jpg/170px-Waseda_University_baseball_players.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Waseda_University_baseball_players.jpg/255px-Waseda_University_baseball_players.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Waseda_University_baseball_players.jpg/340px-Waseda_University_baseball_players.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Waseda_University_baseball_players.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Two players on the baseball team of Tokyo, Japan's <a href="/wiki/Waseda_University" title="Waseda University">Waseda University</a> in 1921</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Afghan_girl_playing_baseball_in_2002.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2100" data-file-width="1500" height="238" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Afghan_girl_playing_baseball_in_2002.jpg/170px-Afghan_girl_playing_baseball_in_2002.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Afghan_girl_playing_baseball_in_2002.jpg/255px-Afghan_girl_playing_baseball_in_2002.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Afghan_girl_playing_baseball_in_2002.jpg/340px-Afghan_girl_playing_baseball_in_2002.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Afghan_girl_playing_baseball_in_2002.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
An Afghan girl playing baseball in August 2002</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Writing in 1919, philosopher <a href="/wiki/Morris_Raphael_Cohen" title="Morris Raphael Cohen">Morris Raphael Cohen</a> described baseball as America's national religion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup> In the words of sports columnist <a href="/wiki/Jayson_Stark" title="Jayson Stark">Jayson Stark</a>, baseball has long been "a unique paragon of American culture"—a status he sees as devastated by the steroid abuse scandal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup> Baseball has an important place in other national cultures as well: Scholar Peter Bjarkman describes "how deeply the sport is ingrained in the history and culture of a nation such as Cuba, [and] how thoroughly it was radically reshaped and nativized in Japan."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup> Since the early 1980s, the Dominican Republic, in particular the city of <a href="/wiki/San_Pedro_de_Macor%C3%ADs" title="San Pedro de Macorís">San Pedro de Macorís</a>, has been the major leagues' primary source of foreign talent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup> Hall-of-Famer <a href="/wiki/Roberto_Clemente" title="Roberto Clemente">Roberto Clemente</a> remains one of the greatest national heroes in Puerto Rico's history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup> While baseball has long been the island's primary athletic pastime, its once well-attended professional winter league has declined in popularity since 1990, when young Puerto Rican players began to be included in the major leagues' annual first-year player draft.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup> In the Western Hemisphere, baseball is also one of the leading sports in Canada, <a href="/wiki/Colombia" title="Colombia">Colombia</a>, Mexico, the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands_Antilles" title="Netherlands Antilles">Netherlands Antilles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nicaragua" title="Nicaragua">Nicaragua</a>, <a href="/wiki/Panama" title="Panama">Panama</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Venezuela" title="Venezuela">Venezuela</a>. In Asia, it is among the most popular sports in Japan, South Korea and Taiwan.</p>
<p>The major league game in the United States was originally targeted toward a middle-class, white-collar audience: relative to other spectator pastimes, the National League's set ticket price of 50 cents in 1876 was high, while the location of playing fields outside the inner city and the workweek daytime scheduling of games were also obstacles to a blue-collar audience.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup> A century later, the situation was very different. With the rise in popularity of other team sports with much higher average ticket prices—football, basketball, and hockey—professional baseball had become among the most blue-collar-oriented of leading American spectator sports.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the late 1900s and early 2000s, baseball's position compared to football in the United States moved in contradictory directions. In 2008, Major League Baseball set a revenue record of $6.5 billion, matching the NFL's revenue for the first time in decades.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup> A new MLB revenue record of $6.6 billion was set in 2009.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brown10_166-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brown10-166">[166]</a></sup> On the other hand, the percentage of American sports fans polled who named baseball as their favorite sport was 16%, compared to pro football at 31%. In 1985, the respective figures were pro football 24%, baseball 23%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup> Because there are so many more major league baseball games played, there is no comparison in overall attendance. In 2008, total attendance at major league games was the second-highest in history: 78.6 million, 0.7% off the record set the previous year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MajorAtRec_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-MajorAtRec-67">[67]</a></sup> The following year, amid the U.S. recession, attendance fell by 6.6% to 73.4 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brown10_166-1"><a href="#cite_note-Brown10-166">[166]</a></sup> Attendance at games held under the <a href="/wiki/Minor_League_Baseball" title="Minor League Baseball">Minor League Baseball</a> umbrella also set a record in 2007, with 42.8 million;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MinorAtRec_68-1"><a href="#cite_note-MinorAtRec-68">[68]</a></sup> this figure does not include attendance at games of the several independent minor leagues.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Opening_Day_of_1961_Baseball_Season._President_Kennedy_throws_out_first_ball._(first_row)_Vice_President_Johnson..._-_NARA_-_194197.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2700" data-file-width="2700" height="240" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Opening_Day_of_1961_Baseball_Season._President_Kennedy_throws_out_first_ball._%28first_row%29_Vice_President_Johnson..._-_NARA_-_194197.jpg/240px-Opening_Day_of_1961_Baseball_Season._President_Kennedy_throws_out_first_ball._%28first_row%29_Vice_President_Johnson..._-_NARA_-_194197.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Opening_Day_of_1961_Baseball_Season._President_Kennedy_throws_out_first_ball._%28first_row%29_Vice_President_Johnson..._-_NARA_-_194197.jpg/360px-Opening_Day_of_1961_Baseball_Season._President_Kennedy_throws_out_first_ball._%28first_row%29_Vice_President_Johnson..._-_NARA_-_194197.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Opening_Day_of_1961_Baseball_Season._President_Kennedy_throws_out_first_ball._%28first_row%29_Vice_President_Johnson..._-_NARA_-_194197.jpg/480px-Opening_Day_of_1961_Baseball_Season._President_Kennedy_throws_out_first_ball._%28first_row%29_Vice_President_Johnson..._-_NARA_-_194197.jpg 2x" width="240"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Opening_Day_of_1961_Baseball_Season._President_Kennedy_throws_out_first_ball._(first_row)_Vice_President_Johnson..._-_NARA_-_194197.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Opening day of 1961 baseball season. President <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy" title="John F. Kennedy">John F. Kennedy</a> throws out first ball.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In Japan, where baseball is inarguably the leading spectator team sport, combined revenue for the twelve teams in <a href="/wiki/Nippon_Professional_Baseball" title="Nippon Professional Baseball">Nippon Professional Baseball</a> (NPB), the body that oversees both the Central and Pacific leagues, was estimated at $1 billion in 2007. Total NPB attendance for the year was approximately 20 million. While in the preceding two decades, MLB attendance grew by 50 percent and revenue nearly tripled, the comparable NPB figures were stagnant. There are concerns that MLB's growing interest in acquiring star Japanese players will hurt the game in their home country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RW_80-1"><a href="#cite_note-RW-80">[80]</a></sup> In Cuba, where baseball is by every reckoning the national sport,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup> the national team overshadows the city and provincial teams that play in the top-level domestic leagues.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WW_169-0"><a href="#cite_note-WW-169">[169]</a></sup> Revenue figures are not released for the country's amateur system. Similarly, according to one official pronouncement, the sport's governing authority "has never taken into account attendance ... because its greatest interest has always been the development of athletes".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[170]</a></sup></p>
<p>As of 2007, Little League Baseball oversees more than 7,000 children's baseball leagues with more than 2.2 million participants–2.1 million in the United States and 123,000 in other countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MB_171-0"><a href="#cite_note-MB-171">[171]</a></sup> Babe Ruth League teams have over 1 million participants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[172]</a></sup> According to the president of the International Baseball Federation, between 300,000 and 500,000 women and girls play baseball around the world, including Little League and the introductory game of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tee_Ball" title="Tee Ball">Tee Ball</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[173]</a></sup></p>
<p>A varsity baseball team is an established part of <a href="/wiki/Physical_education" title="Physical education">physical education</a> departments at most high schools and colleges in the United States. In 2008, nearly half a million high schoolers and over 35,000 collegians played on their schools' baseball teams.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MB_171-1"><a href="#cite_note-MB-171">[171]</a></sup> The number of Americans participating in baseball has declined since the late 1980s, falling well behind the number of soccer participants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[174]</a></sup> By early in the 20th century, intercollegiate baseball was Japan's leading sport. Today, <a href="/wiki/High_school_baseball_in_Japan" title="High school baseball in Japan">high school baseball</a> in particular is immensely popular there.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[175]</a></sup> The final rounds of the two annual tournaments—the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_High_School_Baseball_Invitational_Tournament" title="National High School Baseball Invitational Tournament">National High School Baseball Invitational Tournament</a> in the spring, and the even more important <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_High_School_Baseball_Championship" title="National High School Baseball Championship">National High School Baseball Championship</a> in the summer—are broadcast around the country. The tournaments are known, respectively, as Spring Koshien and Summer Koshien after the <a href="/wiki/Koshien_Stadium" title="Koshien Stadium">55,000-capacity stadium</a> where they are played.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[176]</a></sup> In Cuba, baseball is a mandatory part of the state system of physical education, which begins at age six. Talented children as young as seven are sent to special district schools for more intensive training—the first step on a ladder whose acme is the national baseball team.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WW_169-1"><a href="#cite_note-WW-169">[169]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Baseball_in_popular_culture">Baseball in popular culture</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:HonusWagnerCard.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1501" data-file-width="843" height="392" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/HonusWagnerCard.jpg/220px-HonusWagnerCard.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/HonusWagnerCard.jpg/330px-HonusWagnerCard.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/HonusWagnerCard.jpg/440px-HonusWagnerCard.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:HonusWagnerCard.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/American_Tobacco_Company" title="American Tobacco Company">American Tobacco Company</a>'s line of baseball cards featured shortstop <a href="/wiki/Honus_Wagner" title="Honus Wagner">Honus Wagner</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Pittsburgh_Pirates" title="Pittsburgh Pirates">Pittsburgh Pirates</a> from 1909 to 1911. In 2007, <a href="/wiki/T206_Honus_Wagner" title="T206 Honus Wagner">the card shown here</a> sold for $2.8 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[177]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Baseball has had a broad impact on popular culture, both in the United States and elsewhere. Dozens of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/English_language_idioms_derived_from_baseball" title="English language idioms derived from baseball">English-language idioms have been derived from baseball</a>; in particular, the game is the source of a number of widely used <a href="/wiki/Baseball_metaphors_for_sex" title="Baseball metaphors for sex">sexual euphemisms</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[178]</a></sup> The first networked radio broadcasts in North America were of the <a href="/wiki/1922_World_Series" title="1922 World Series">1922 World Series</a>: famed sportswriter <a href="/wiki/Grantland_Rice" title="Grantland Rice">Grantland Rice</a> announced <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Play-by-play" title="Play-by-play">play-by-play</a> from New York City's <a href="/wiki/Polo_Grounds" title="Polo Grounds">Polo Grounds</a> on <a href="/wiki/WABC_(AM)#As_WJZ" title="WABC (AM)">WJZ</a>–<a href="/wiki/Newark,_New_Jersey" title="Newark, New Jersey">Newark, New Jersey</a>, which was connected by wire to <a href="/wiki/WGY_(AM)" title="WGY (AM)">WGY</a>–<a href="/wiki/Schenectady,_New_York" title="Schenectady, New York">Schenectady, New York</a>, and <a href="/wiki/WBZ_(AM)" title="WBZ (AM)">WBZ</a>–<a href="/wiki/Springfield,_Massachusetts" title="Springfield, Massachusetts">Springfield, Massachusetts</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[179]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Baseball_cap" title="Baseball cap">baseball cap</a> has become a ubiquitous fashion item not only in the United States and Japan, but also in countries where the sport itself is not particularly popular, such as the United Kingdom.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[180]</a></sup></p>
<p>Baseball has inspired many works of art and entertainment. One of the first major examples, <a href="/wiki/Ernest_Thayer" title="Ernest Thayer">Ernest Thayer</a>'s poem "<a href="/wiki/Casey_at_the_Bat" title="Casey at the Bat">Casey at the Bat</a>", appeared in 1888. A wry description of the failure of a star player in what would now be called a "clutch situation", the poem became the source of <a href="/wiki/Vaudeville" title="Vaudeville">vaudeville</a> and other staged performances, audio recordings, film adaptations, and an opera, as well as a host of sequels and parodies in various media. There have been many <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baseball_movie" title="Baseball movie">baseball movies</a>, including the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Academy_Award" title="Academy Award">Academy Award</a>–winning <i><a href="/wiki/The_Pride_of_the_Yankees" title="The Pride of the Yankees">The Pride of the Yankees</a></i> (1942) and the Oscar nominees <i><a href="/wiki/The_Natural_(film)" title="The Natural (film)">The Natural</a></i> (1984) and <i><a href="/wiki/Field_of_Dreams" title="Field of Dreams">Field of Dreams</a></i> (1989). The <a href="/wiki/American_Film_Institute" title="American Film Institute">American Film Institute</a>'s selection of the ten best sports movies includes <i>The Pride of the Yankees</i> at number 3 and <i><a href="/wiki/Bull_Durham" title="Bull Durham">Bull Durham</a></i> (1988) at number 5.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181"><a href="#cite_note-181">[181]</a></sup> Baseball has provided thematic material for hits on both stage—the <a href="/wiki/Richard_Adler" title="Richard Adler">Adler</a>–<a href="/wiki/Jerry_Ross_(composer)" title="Jerry Ross (composer)">Ross</a> musical <i><a href="/wiki/Damn_Yankees" title="Damn Yankees">Damn Yankees</a></i>—and record—<a href="/wiki/George_J._Gaskin" title="George J. Gaskin">George J. Gaskin</a>'s "Slide, Kelly, Slide", <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Simon_and_Garfunkel" title="Simon and Garfunkel">Simon and Garfunkel</a>'s "<a href="/wiki/Mrs._Robinson" title="Mrs. Robinson">Mrs. Robinson</a>", and <a href="/wiki/John_Fogerty" title="John Fogerty">John Fogerty</a>'s "<a href="/wiki/Centerfield_(song)" title="Centerfield (song)">Centerfield</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182"><a href="#cite_note-182">[182]</a></sup> The baseball-inspired comedic sketch "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Who%27s_on_First" title="Who's on First">Who's on First</a>", popularized by <a href="/wiki/Abbott_and_Costello" title="Abbott and Costello">Abbott and Costello</a> in 1938, quickly became famous. Six decades later, <i><a href="/wiki/Time_(magazine)" title="Time (magazine)">Time</a></i> named it the best comedy routine of the 20th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183"><a href="#cite_note-183">[183]</a></sup> Baseball is also featured in various video games including <i><a href="/wiki/MLB:_The_Show" title="MLB: The Show">MLB: The Show</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Wii_Sports" title="Wii Sports">Wii Sports</a></i>, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kinect_Sports:_Season_2" title="Kinect Sports: Season 2">Kinect Sports: Season 2</a></i> and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mario_Baseball" title="Mario Baseball">Mario Baseball</a></i>.</p>
<p>Literary works connected to the game include the short fiction of <a href="/wiki/Ring_Lardner" title="Ring Lardner">Ring Lardner</a> and novels such as <a href="/wiki/Bernard_Malamud" title="Bernard Malamud">Bernard Malamud</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Natural" title="The Natural">The Natural</a></i> (the source for the movie), <a href="/wiki/Robert_Coover" title="Robert Coover">Robert Coover</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Universal_Baseball_Association,_Inc.,_J._Henry_Waugh,_Prop." title="The Universal Baseball Association, Inc., J. Henry Waugh, Prop.">The Universal Baseball Association, Inc., J. Henry Waugh, Prop.</a></i>, and <a href="/wiki/W._P._Kinsella" title="W. P. Kinsella">W. P. Kinsella</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Shoeless_Joe_(novel)" title="Shoeless Joe (novel)">Shoeless Joe</a></i> (the source for <i>Field of Dreams</i>). Baseball's literary canon also includes the beat reportage of <a href="/wiki/Damon_Runyon" title="Damon Runyon">Damon Runyon</a>; the columns of Grantland Rice, <a href="/wiki/Red_Smith_(sportswriter)" title="Red Smith (sportswriter)">Red Smith</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dick_Young_(sportswriter)" title="Dick Young (sportswriter)">Dick Young</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Peter_Gammons" title="Peter Gammons">Peter Gammons</a>; and the essays of <a href="/wiki/Roger_Angell" title="Roger Angell">Roger Angell</a>. Among the celebrated nonfiction books in the field are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lawrence_S._Ritter" title="Lawrence S. Ritter">Lawrence S. Ritter</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Glory_of_Their_Times" title="The Glory of Their Times">The Glory of Their Times</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Roger_Kahn" title="Roger Kahn">Roger Kahn</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Boys_of_Summer_(book)" title="The Boys of Summer (book)">The Boys of Summer</a></i>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Michael_Lewis_(author)" title="Michael Lewis (author)">Michael Lewis</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Moneyball" title="Moneyball">Moneyball</a></i>. The 1970 publication of major league pitcher <a href="/wiki/Jim_Bouton" title="Jim Bouton">Jim Bouton</a>'s tell-all chronicle <i><a href="/wiki/Ball_Four" title="Ball Four">Ball Four</a></i> is considered a turning point in the reporting of professional sports.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184"><a href="#cite_note-184">[184]</a></sup></p>
<p>Baseball has also inspired the creation of new cultural forms. <a href="/wiki/Baseball_card" title="Baseball card">Baseball cards</a> were introduced in the late 19th century as <a href="/wiki/Trade_card" title="Trade card">trade cards</a>. A typical example would feature an image of a baseball player on one side and advertising for a business on the other. In the early 1900s they were produced widely as promotional items by tobacco and confectionery companies. The 1930s saw the popularization of the modern style of baseball card, with a player photograph accompanied on the rear by statistics and biographical data. Baseball cards—many of which are now prized collectibles—are the source of the much broader <a href="/wiki/Trading_card" title="Trading card">trading card</a> industry, involving similar products for different sports and non-sports-related fields.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[185]</a></sup></p>
<p>Modern <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fantasy_sports" title="Fantasy sports">fantasy sports</a> began in 1980 with the invention of <a href="/wiki/Fantasy_baseball#Rotisserie_League_Baseball" title="Fantasy baseball">Rotisserie League Baseball</a> by New York writer <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Okrent" title="Daniel Okrent">Daniel Okrent</a> and several friends. Participants in a Rotisserie league draft notional teams from the list of active Major League Baseball players and play out an entire imaginary season with game outcomes based on the players' latest real-world statistics. Rotisserie-style play quickly became a phenomenon. Now known more generically as <a href="/wiki/Fantasy_baseball" title="Fantasy baseball">fantasy baseball</a>, it has inspired similar games based on an array of different sports.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[186]</a></sup> The field boomed with increasing Internet access and new fantasy sports–related websites. By 2008, 29.9 million people in the United States and Canada were playing fantasy sports, spending $800 million on the hobby.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187"><a href="#cite_note-187">[187]</a></sup> The burgeoning popularity of fantasy baseball is also credited with the increasing attention paid to sabermetrics—first among fans, only later among baseball professionals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[188]</a></sup></p>

<li id="cite_note-131"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-131">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Morton, Richard, "Baseball in England", <i>Badminton Magazine</i>, August 1896, pp. 157–158: "The scoring is one of the most interesting features in this new importation from America [baseball]. Every detail of play is recorded, and a man's mistakes are tabulated as well as his successes ... A line in a cricket score may read, 'Lockwood, <i>caught</i> Stoddart, <i>bowled</i> J. T. Hearne; 30.' ... [T]here is so much that is left out! There is no mention of the fact that O'Brien missed Lockwood before he had scored, and that somebody else failed to take a chance when his score was ten. These are items that go to make cricket history; but there is no record of them in the analysis ... The man who catches a ball is thought worthy of mention, but the man who muffs one does not suffer by publicity."</span></li>
